An appositive is a noun or noun phrase that renames or identifies another noun. Non-essential appositives are set off by commas.

> *Marie Curie, a pioneering physicist, won two Nobel prizes.*
> *My brother, who lives in Seattle, is visiting next week.* (I have only one brother — the clause is non-essential.)

> *My brother who lives in Seattle is visiting next week.* (I have multiple brothers — the clause identifies WHICH brother. Essential, no commas.)

### 3.1.6 Comma Rule #5: Between Coordinate Adjectives

When two adjectives independently modify the same noun and can be separated by "and" or reordered without changing the meaning, a comma goes between them. These are called **coordinate adjectives**.

**Test for Coordinate Adjectives:**
1. Can you insert "and" between them? (*a talented, dedicated teacher* → *a talented and dedicated teacher* ✓)
2. Can you reverse their order? (*a dedicated, talented teacher* — still makes sense ✓)

If both tests work, they are coordinate, and a comma is required:
> *She is a brilliant, innovative scientist.*
> *The old, crumbling building was condemned.*

**Cumulative Adjectives (NO comma):**
When adjectives build on each other (the first adjective modifies the combination of the second adjective + noun), they are cumulative and do NOT take a comma.

> *She drove a small red car.* (NOT *small, red car* — "red car" is a unit, and "small" modifies that unit.)
> *He wore a bright blue suit.* (NOT *bright, blue suit*)

**Test:** Can you insert "and"? "Small and red car" sounds slightly off. Can you reverse it? "Red small car" is clearly wrong. They are cumulative—no comma.

**Key Nuance:** When in doubt, apply the "and" test. If "and" sounds unnatural, omit the comma. The SAT tests this distinction directly, and the difference can change meaning subtly.

### 3.1.7 Comma Rule #6: Setting Off Transitional Expressions and Parenthetical Elements within Sentences

Transitional expressions that interrupt the flow of a sentence are set off by commas:

> *The results, however, were inconclusive.*
> *The data, therefore, cannot be trusted.*
> *She was, in fact, the most qualified candidate.*
> *His conclusion, it must be said, was premature.*

These are often called "parenthetical commas" because they function similarly to parentheses—they set off information that could be removed without destroying the sentence's grammatical integrity.

### 3.1.8 Comma Rule #7: In Dates, Addresses, and Titles

**Dates:**
> *The conference was held on March 15, 2024, in Boston.* (Comma after the year when the sentence continues)
> *July 4, 1776, is a significant date.*

**Addresses:**
> *They moved to Austin, Texas, last spring.*
> *The office is located at 450 Broadway, Suite 200, New York, New York.*

**Titles and Name Clarifiers:**
> *Sarah Jones, M.D., presented the findings.*
> *John Smith, the committee chair, called the meeting to order.*

### 3.1.9 Comma Rule #8: After a Participial Phrase at the End of a Sentence

When a participial phrase or other modifying phrase follows the main clause and provides additional description, it is typically preceded by a comma:

> *She walked out of the room, smiling quietly.*
> *The dog sat by the door, waiting for its owner to return.*

### 3.1.10 When Commas Must NOT Be Used

Understanding comma prohibitions is equally important. The SAT frequently tests prohibited commas by presenting answer choices with commas in incorrect positions.

**Prohibition #1: Never separate a subject from its verb with a comma.**

> ✗ *The collection of rare coins, is on display.*
> ✓ *The collection of rare coins is on display.*

The subject is "collection" (singular), not "coins." Even though a comma might feel natural after the long phrase "of rare coins," it is grammatically wrong. **This is one of the most common errors on the SAT.**

**Critical Strategy:** To find the true subject, cross out all prepositional phrases, relative clauses, and other "filler" between the potential subject and the verb. Then check: does the comma separate what remains?

> *The impact of the new regulations on small businesses has been significant.* (No comma between "impact" and "has.")

**Prohibition #2: Never place a comma before a preposition or after a prepositional phrase when it naturally belongs.**

> ✗ *She walked to, the store.*
> ✗ *She walked, to the store.* (Comma after the verb separating it from the prepositional phrase)

> ✓ *She walked to the store.*

**Prohibition #3: Never use a comma between only two items in a list (the "comma splice of listing").**

> ✗ *The flag is red, and blue.* 
> ✓ *The flag is red and blue.*

A comma is only used in lists of THREE or more items.

**Prohibition #4: Never use a comma alone to join two independent clauses (comma splice).**

> ✗ *She studied hard, she passed the exam.*

This is both a comma prohibition and a run-on sentence issue, which will be covered in detail in the run-on section.

**Prohibition #5: Never use a comma between a verb and its complement or object.**

> ✗ *The study revealed, that the hypothesis was correct.*
> ✓ *The study revealed that the hypothesis was correct.*

> ✗ *She believes, that the theory is sound.*
> ✓ *She believes that the theory is sound.*

---

## 3.2 The Semicolon (;) — The Sentence Bridge

The semicolon is the most misunderstood punctuation mark in English, but its rules are actually quite straightforward and highly testable. On the SAT, semicolon questions appear regularly, and understanding this mark can quickly add points to your score.

### 3.2.1 The Fundamental Rule: Semicolons Join Two Independent Clauses

A semicolon connects two closely related independent clauses without a coordinating conjunction. Both clauses must be able to stand alone as complete sentences.

> *The results were conclusive; the team published their findings immediately.*
> *She did not enjoy the lecture; it was disorganized and rambling.*
> *The museum has an impressive collection; visitors can see works from every era.*

**The Test:** Cover the semicolon. On the left side: is there a subject and a verb that could stand alone as a sentence? On the right side: is there a subject and a verb that could stand alone as a sentence? If both sides pass this test, the semicolon is potentially correct.

### 3.2.2 The Semicolon Must Connect Logically Related Ideas

While this is more of a stylistic guideline than a hard grammatical rule, the SAT expects semicolons to connect ideas that are genuinely related. A semicolon between two random, unrelated sentences would be grammatically acceptable but rhetorically poor.

> ✓ *The storm caused widespread damage; many homes lost power for days.* (Cause-effect — clearly related)
> ✓ *The author published her first novel in 2005; her second, which came out in 2010, won a major award.* (Same topic — clearly related)

### 3.2.3 Semicolon vs. Comma + FANBOYS

A semicolon and a comma-plus-coordinating-conjunction serve the same grammatical function (joining two independent clauses), but they are stylistic choices:

> *She studied diligently, and she earned a high score.* (Comma + FANBOYS)
> *She studied diligently; she earned a high score.* (Semicolon)

Both are correct. The semicolon creates a slightly tighter connection between the two ideas, while the conjunction makes the logical relationship more explicit (in this case, cause-and-effect via "and"). On the SAT, you may see both options in answer choices, and the correct one will depend on other factors like the presence or absence of a conjunction, or the logical flow of the passage.

### 3.2.4 Semicolons in Complex Lists (The Super List Rule)

This is one of the most SAT-specific semicolon applications. When a list contains items that themselves have commas, semicolons replace commas between list items to prevent confusion:

> *The tour included stops in Paris, France; Rome, Italy; and Madrid, Spain.*
> *The committee included Dr. Patel, the chairperson; Ms. Rivera, the treasurer; and Mr. Kim, the secretary.*

Without semicolons, these lists would be impossibly confusing:
> ✗ *The tour included stops in Paris, France, Rome, Italy, and Madrid, Spain.* (How many items? Six? Three?)

**Key Rule:** If any item in a list contains an internal comma, ALL separators in that list should be semicolons, even if some items don't have internal commas.

### 3.2.5 Common Semicolon Errors on the SAT

**Error #1: Using a semicolon where one side is not an independent clause.**

> ✗ *Although the results were positive; the team continued testing.* ("Although the results were positive" is a dependent clause — it cannot stand alone. No semicolon.)

> ✗ *The discovery of penicillin; which saved millions of lives.* (Neither side is an independent clause.)

**Error #2: Using a semicolon with a transitional adverb and comma (the "however" construction).**

> ✗ *The data were inconclusive, however; the team published anyway.* (Mixed punctuation — this is wrong.)

> ✓ *The data were inconclusive; however, the team published anyway.* (Semicolon + transitional adverb + comma — this is the correct pattern.)

**Error #3: Using a semicolon to introduce a list.**

> ✗ *She needed three items; a pen, a notebook, and a calculator.* (Use a colon, not a semicolon, to introduce a list.)

### 3.2.6 The Semicolon + Transitional Adverb Pattern

This construction appears frequently on the SAT and follows a specific pattern:

> **Independent clause; transitional adverb, independent clause.**

> *The hypothesis was sound; however, the methodology was flawed.*
> *The funding was reduced; consequently, the project was delayed.*
> *The initial results were promising; nevertheless, further testing was required.*

**Common transitional adverbs used with semicolons:**
however, therefore, consequently, furthermore, moreover, nevertheless, nonetheless, meanwhile, otherwise, instead, indeed, thus, hence, accordingly, similarly, likewise, specifically, in fact, in addition, in contrast, on the other hand, as a result, for example, for instance, in other words, above all, after all, even so, of course, on the contrary, that is

**Critical Rule:** When you see a semicolon followed by a word like "however" or "therefore," that word MUST be followed by a comma. The pattern is always: **; [transitional adverb],**

> ✗ *The data were flawed; therefore the study was invalid.* (Missing comma after "therefore")
> ✓ *The data were flawed; therefore, the study was invalid.*

---

## 3.3 The Colon (:) — The Introduction Mark

The colon is the most rule-bound punctuation mark on the SAT. Its usage is governed by one absolute requirement, and understanding that requirement eliminates most wrong answers instantly.

### 3.3.1 The Fundamental Rule: The Left Side Must Be a Complete Independent Clause

The clause that precedes a colon MUST be a grammatically complete independent clause. It must have a subject, a verb, and express a complete thought. What follows the colon can be virtually anything: a word, a phrase, a list, a clause, or another complete sentence.

**Correct Examples:**
> *She needed three supplies: a calculator, a pencil, and scratch paper.* (Left side: complete sentence. Right side: list.)
> *The conclusion was clear: the experiment had failed.* (Left side: complete sentence. Right side: explanation.)
> *He had one goal: to win the championship.* (Left side: complete sentence. Right side: infinitive phrase.)
> *The evidence pointed in one direction: the suspect had been at the scene.* (Left side: complete sentence. Right side: complete sentence.)

**Incorrect Examples:**
> ✗ *She needed: a calculator, a pencil, and scratch paper.* (Left side "She needed" is NOT a complete thought — needed what? The sentence is incomplete without what follows.)
> ✗ *The three supplies are: a calculator, a pencil, and scratch paper.* (Left side "The three supplies are" is NOT a complete thought — the verb "are" demands a complement.)
> ✗ *Her goals were: to graduate, to find a job, and to travel.* (Left side "Her goals were" is incomplete.)

**The "As Follows" Test:** If you could naturally insert the words "as follows" or "specifically" before the colon, the colon is likely correct:

> *She needed three supplies, as follows: a calculator, a pencil, and scratch paper.* ✓
> *The conclusion was clear, specifically: the experiment had failed.* ✓

If inserting "as follows" creates an ungrammatical sentence, the colon is wrong.

### 3.3.2 What Follows the Colon

The colon can introduce:

**A list:**
> *The recipe called for three ingredients: flour, sugar, and butter.*

**An explanation or elaboration:**
> *The reason was obvious: she had not prepared.*

**A quotation (when the introduction is a complete clause):**
> *The sign read: "No trespassing."*

**A restatement or amplification:**
> *The verdict was unanimous: all twelve jurors voted to convict.*

**A single word (for emphasis):**
> *There was only one possible explanation: sabotage.*

### 3.3.3 Colon vs. Semicolon — The Critical Distinction

This is one of the most commonly tested punctuation comparisons on the SAT:

| Feature | Semicolon (;) | Colon (:) |
|---|---|---|
| **Left side requirement** | Must be a complete independent clause | Must be a complete independent clause |
| **Right side requirement** | Must be a complete independent clause | Can be anything (word, phrase, list, clause) |
| **Function** | Connects two related complete thoughts | Introduces what follows |

> *She was tired; she went to bed early.* (Both sides are complete sentences — semicolon.)
> *She had one goal: to finish the marathon.* (Right side is not a complete sentence — colon.)
> *She had one goal: she wanted to finish the marathon.* (Both sides are complete sentences — colon is acceptable because the right side explains the left.)

### 3.3.4 Colon After a Verb or Preposition — Always Wrong

Because the left side of a colon must be a complete independent clause, a colon immediately after a verb or preposition (which would make the left side incomplete) is always incorrect:

> ✗ *The countries visited were: France, Spain, and Italy.* ("The countries visited were" is incomplete.)
> ✓ *The countries visited were France, Spain, and Italy.* (No colon needed.)
> ✓ *They visited three countries: France, Spain, and Italy.* (Left side "They visited three countries" is complete.)

> ✗ *She was interested in: biology, chemistry, and physics.* ("She was interested in" is incomplete.)
> ✓ *She was interested in biology, chemistry, and physics.* (No colon needed.)
> ✓ *She was interested in three fields: biology, chemistry, and physics.* (Left side is complete.)

---

## 3.4 The Em Dash (—) — The Emphatic Separator

The em dash is a versatile punctuation mark that serves as a more emphatic alternative to commas or parentheses. On the SAT, dash questions appear less frequently than comma or semicolon questions, but they are still tested and understanding the rules is essential.

### 3.4.1 The Fundamental Rule: Dashes Must Come in Pairs (Usually)

When em dashes set off a non-essential element in the middle of a sentence, they must appear in pairs—one before and one after the element:

> *The scientist—a renowned botanist—discovered the new species.*
> *The city—once a small village—has grown significantly.*
> *His opinion—which I respect—differs from mine.*

**Matching Punctuation Rule:** You cannot mix dashes with commas or parentheses. If you open with a dash, you must close with a dash. If you open with a comma, you must close with a comma.

> ✗ *The scientist—a renowned botanist, discovered the new species.* (Mixed — dash and comma)
> ✗ *The scientist, a renowned botanist—discovered the new species.* (Mixed — comma and dash)
> ✓ *The scientist—a renowned botanist—discovered the new species.* (Matching dashes)
> ✓ *The scientist, a renowned botanist, discovered the new species.* (Matching commas)
> ✓ *The scientist (a renowned botanist) discovered the new species.* (Matching parentheses)

### 3.4.2 Single Em Dash — Before an Explanation or Dramatic Element

A single em dash can appear at the end of a sentence to introduce an explanation, summary, or dramatic/shocking piece of information. In this usage, the single dash functions similarly to a colon but with more emphasis:

> *She had one goal—to win the championship.*
> *The investigation revealed one truth—the system had failed.*
> *He opened the door and saw something unexpected—an empty room.*

**Colon vs. Dash:** Both can introduce explanations. The dash is more emphatic and informal. On the SAT, if both a colon and a dash are options and the left side is a complete independent clause, the choice between them often depends on whether the right side is a complete sentence (colon) or a phrase/fragment (dash), or on the degree of emphasis intended.

### 3.4.3 Em Dash as an Alternative to Parentheses

Em dashes can set off non-essential information, just like parentheses, but with a key difference: parentheses signal that the information is truly supplementary (almost an afterthought), while dashes signal that the information is more important and deserves emphasis:

> *The results—which surprised everyone—were published immediately.* (Emphasizes the surprise)
> *The results (which surprised everyone) were published immediately.* (Downplays the surprise)

On the SAT, the choice between dashes, commas, and parentheses for setting off non-essential information is sometimes tested, and the correct answer depends on the context and the degree of emphasis the passage intends.

### 3.4.4 Em Dash to Indicate an Interruption or Abrupt Change

> *"I was just trying to—" "I don't want to hear it," she interrupted.*

This usage is rare on the SAT but worth noting for completeness.

### 3.4.5 Common Dash Errors on the SAT

**Error #1: Mixing dashes with other punctuation.**
> ✗ *The city—once a small village, has grown significantly.*
> ✓ *The city—once a small village—has grown significantly.*

**Error #2: Using only one dash when a pair is needed.**
> ✗ *The scientist—a renowned botanist discovered the new species.* (Missing closing dash)
> ✓ *The scientist—a renowned botanist—discovered the new species.*

**Error #3: Using dashes to separate a subject from its verb.**
> ✗ *The collection—of rare coins—is on display.* (Dashes here are wrong; no punctuation should separate subject from verb.)
> ✓ *The collection of rare coins is on display.*

---

## 3.5 The Apostrophe (') — Contraction vs. Possession

Apostrophe questions on the SAT test two primary skills: distinguishing possessives from contractions, and applying the correct possessive form to singular nouns, plural nouns, and irregular plurals.

### 3.5.1 The Two Functions of the Apostrophe

**Function #1: Contraction**
An apostrophe replaces missing letters in a contracted form:

| Contraction | Full Form |
|---|---|
| it's | it is / it has |
| they're | they are |
| you're | you are |
| we're | we are |
| he's | he is / he has |
| she's | she is / she has |
| who's | who is / who has |
| there's | there is / there has |
| that's | that is / that has |
| let's | let us |
| would've | would have |
| could've | could have |
| should've | should have |

**Function #2: Possession**
An apostrophe indicates that something belongs to someone or something:

> *the student's essay* (the essay belonging to one student)
> *the students' essays* (the essays belonging to multiple students)
> *the dog's bone* (the bone belonging to one dog)
> *the dogs' bones* (the bones belonging to multiple dogs)

### 3.5.2 The Substitution Test — The Most Reliable Apostrophe Strategy

When you see an apostrophe in an answer choice, apply the substitution test:

- If the word can be expanded to its full form (e.g., *it's* → *it is*), the apostrophe is correct for a contraction.
- If the word cannot be expanded but shows ownership, the apostrophe indicates possession.

> *The dog wagged its tail.* → Can you say "The dog wagged it is tail"? NO. So "its" is possessive — no apostrophe.
> *It's raining.* → Can you say "It is raining"? YES. So "it's" is a contraction — apostrophe needed.

> *They took their seats.* → Can you say "They took they are seats"? NO. So "their" is possessive — no apostrophe.
> *They're ready.* → Can you say "They are ready"? YES. So "they're" is a contraction — apostrophe needed.

### 3.5.3 Possessive Rules by Noun Type

**Singular Nouns: Add 's**
> *the cat's toy*
> *the professor's lecture*
> *James's car* (or *James' car* — both are accepted, but the SAT prefers 's for singular proper nouns)
> *the witness's testimony*

**Singular Nouns Ending in "s": Add 's (generally)**
> *the boss's office*
> *the class's assignment*
> *Chris's notebook*

Note: Some style guides allow just an apostrophe for singular proper nouns ending in "s" (e.g., *James' car*), but the SAT generally prefers 's and will not test this distinction as the sole differentiator between answer choices.

**Plural Nouns Ending in "s": Add only an apostrophe after the s**
> *the students' essays* (multiple students)
> *the teachers' lounge* (multiple teachers)
> *the dogs' bones* (multiple dogs)
> *the companies' policies* (multiple companies)

**Irregular Plural Nouns (not ending in "s"): Add 's**
> *the children's toys* (children is already plural but doesn't end in s)
> *the women's department*
> *the people's choice*
> *the mice's nest*
> *the geese's migration*

This is a frequently tested distinction. Students often write *childrens'* instead of *children's* because they forget that "children" is already plural.

### 3.5.4 Possessive Pronouns — NEVER Use Apostrophes

Possessive pronouns are inherently possessive and never take apostrophes:

| Possessive Pronoun | Common Error |
|---|---|
| its | ~~it's~~ (this means "it is") |
| theirs | ~~their's~~ (this word does not exist) |
| hers | ~~her's~~ (this means "her is" — which makes no sense) |
| yours | ~~your's~~ (this word does not exist) |
| ours | ~~our's~~ (this word does not exist) |
| whose | ~~who's~~ (this means "who is") |

**The SAT loves to test this.** If you see "it's" vs. "its" or "whose" vs. "who's" in answer choices, the substitution test will always give you the correct answer.

### 3.5.5 Apostrophes with Plurals — When NOT to Use Them

**Never use an apostrophe to form the plural of a noun:**

> ✗ *The 1990's were a transformative decade.* → ✓ *The 1990s were a transformative decade.*
> ✗ *She has two cat's.* → ✓ *She has two cats.*
> ✗ *The student's studied hard.* (if you mean multiple students studied) → ✓ *The students studied hard.*

**Exception:** Use an apostrophe to form the plural of single lowercase letters to avoid confusion:
> *Mind your p's and q's.* (Without apostrophes, "ps" and "qs" would be confusing.)
> *She received all A's this semester.* (Without apostrophe, "As" could be confused with the word "as.")

This exception is rarely tested but worth knowing.

### 3.5.6 Joint Possession vs. Separate Possession

**Joint Possession (two people share ownership of the same thing): Add 's only to the last name:**
> *Smith and Wesson's factory* (one factory they share)
> *Jack and Jill's project* (one project they share)

**Separate Possession (two people each own different things): Add 's to each name:**
> *Smith's and Wesson's factories* (each has a different factory)
> *Jack's and Jill's projects* (each has a different project)

### 3.5.7 Apostrophes in Compound Nouns

For compound nouns, add 's to the last word:
> *the mother-in-law's house*
> *the attorney general's decision*
> *the editor-in-chief's office*

---

## 3.6 Punctuation Combinations and Interactions

The SAT frequently tests how different punctuation marks interact within a single sentence. Understanding these combinations is essential.

### 3.6.1 Commas and Semicolons Together

When a sentence contains both a list with internal commas and independent clauses, semicolons separate the list items while commas serve their usual functions:

> *The conference featured speakers from Paris, France; Berlin, Germany; and Tokyo, Japan.*

> *The committee included Dr. Patel, the chairperson; Ms. Rivera, the treasurer; and Mr. Kim, the secretary; they met every Tuesday.*

### 3.6.2 Colons and Commas

A colon can introduce a list that contains commas:

> *She packed the essentials: a toothbrush, a change of clothes, and her passport.*

No comma should follow the colon (this is a common error in student writing).

### 3.6.3 Dashes and Commas

When a non-essential element appears at the end of a sentence, a single dash can replace the opening comma:

> *She found three things in the attic—old photographs, a dusty journal, and a faded map.*
> *She found three things in the attic, including old photographs, a dusty journal, and a faded map.* (No dash needed with "including")

### 3.6.4 Quotation Marks and Other Punctuation

While the SAT does not test quotation mark rules extensively, it is important to know that in American English, commas and periods go INSIDE closing quotation marks:

> *"Let's begin," she said.*
> *He described the results as "groundbreaking."*

Semicolons and colons go OUTSIDE closing quotation marks:

> *She described the findings as "groundbreaking"; however, others disagreed.*

Question marks and exclamation points go inside if they are part of the quoted material, outside if they are not:

> *She asked, "When will the results be published?"*
> *Did she really describe the findings as "groundbreaking"?*

---

## 3.7 The "No Punctuation" Option — When Less Is More

On many SAT punctuation questions, one of the answer choices will be "no punctuation" (i.e., the blank has no mark at all). This is frequently the correct answer. The SAT values concision and clarity, and unnecessary punctuation is just as wrong as missing punctuation.

**When no punctuation is needed:**

1. **Between a subject and its verb:** *The collection of rare coins is on display.* (No comma between "coins" and "is.")
2. **Between a verb and its object:** *She believes that the theory is sound.* (No comma before "that.")
3. **Between a verb and its complement:** *He became a doctor.* (No comma.)
4. **Before a preposition:** *She walked to the store.* (No comma before "to.")
5. **Between two items joined by "and":** *She studied algebra and geometry.* (No comma.)
6. **After a coordinating conjunction:** *She studied hard, and she passed.* (Comma before "and," not after.)

---

## 3.8 Comprehensive Punctuation Decision Framework

When you encounter a punctuation question on the SAT, follow this systematic process:

**Step 1: Identify what the punctuation separates or introduces.**
- Two independent clauses? → Consider period, semicolon, or comma + FANBOYS.
- An introductory element and the main clause? → Comma.
- A non-essential element within a sentence? → Matching pair (commas, dashes, or parentheses).
- A list or explanation after a complete sentence? → Colon.
- Items in a list? → Commas (or semicolons if items contain internal commas).

**Step 2: Check the left side of the punctuation mark.**
- Is it a complete independent clause? (Required for colons and semicolons.)
- Is it an introductory element? (Requires a comma after it.)

**Step 3: Check the right side of the punctuation mark.**
- Is it a complete independent clause? (Required for semicolons; optional for colons.)
- Is it a list, phrase, or word? (Acceptable after a colon; not after a semicolon.)

**Step 4: Apply the specific rules for the punctuation mark in question.**
- Comma rules (8 rules, plus prohibitions).
- Semicolon rules (independent clauses on both sides; complex lists).
- Colon rules (complete sentence on the left; anything on the right).
- Dash rules (matching pairs; single dash for emphasis).
- Apostrophe rules (possession vs. contraction; substitution test).

**Step 5: Eliminate answer choices that violate the applicable rule.**
- One choice will be clean and correct; others will each break a specific rule.

---

## 3.9 Punctuation and Sentence Structure: The Big Picture

Punctuation does not exist in isolation—it is a reflection of sentence structure. Every punctuation mark encodes a structural relationship between parts of a sentence. Understanding these relationships is the key to mastering punctuation questions.

**Structural Hierarchy of Punctuation (from weakest to strongest separation):**

1. **Comma (,):** Separates items, sets off non-essential elements, marks boundaries between clauses.
2. **Semicolon (;):** Separates two independent clauses of equal weight.
3. **Colon (:):** Separates an introduction from what follows (list, explanation, elaboration).
4. **Em Dash (—):** Separates non-essential elements with emphasis; or introduces a dramatic explanation.
5. **Period (.):** Separates two independent clauses into two sentences.

**The key insight:** The strength of the punctuation should match the degree of separation between the ideas. Two closely related independent clauses get a semicolon. Two independent clauses that could stand alone as separate sentences get a period. An introduction followed by a list gets a colon. A non-essential interruption gets commas, dashes, or parentheses.

---

## 3.10 Common Punctuation Traps on the SAT — A Comprehensive Catalog

### Trap #1: The Subject-Verb Separator
The SAT frequently places a comma between a subject and its verb, especially when the subject is long or modified by a prepositional phrase:

> ✗ *The impact of the new regulations on small businesses, has been significant.*
> ✓ *The impact of the new regulations on small businesses has been significant.*

**Defense:** Cross out everything between the subject and verb. If the remaining words form a complete subject-verb pair, no comma belongs between them.

### Trap #2: The Verb-Object Separator
Similarly, a comma sometimes appears between a verb and its object or complement:

> ✗ *The study revealed, that the hypothesis was incorrect.*
> ✓ *The study revealed that the hypothesis was incorrect.*

**Defense:** "That" clauses that serve as objects of verbs are not set off by commas.

### Trap #3: The Two-Item "List" Comma
A comma appears between only two items joined by "and" or "or":

> ✗ *She studied algebra, and geometry.*
> ✓ *She studied algebra and geometry.*

**Defense:** Commas separate items in lists of THREE or more.

### Trap #4: The Comma Splice Disguised as a Correct Answer
Two independent clauses are joined by only a comma:

> ✗ *The experiment was successful, the results exceeded expectations.*

**Defense:** Two independent clauses need a period, semicolon, or comma + FANBOYS. A comma alone is never sufficient.

### Trap #5: The Colon After an Incomplete Sentence
A colon appears after a phrase that is not a complete independent clause:

> ✗ *The three priorities are: growth, innovation, and sustainability.*
> ✓ *The three priorities are growth, innovation, and sustainability.*
> ✓ *She identified three priorities: growth, innovation, and sustainability.*

**Defense:** Read what comes before the colon. If it's not a complete sentence, the colon is wrong.

### Trap #6: The Unmatched Dash
A dash appears on only one side of a non-essential element:

> ✗ *The scientist—a renowned botanist, made a breakthrough.*
> ✓ *The scientist—a renowned botanist—made a breakthrough.*

**Defense:** Dashes that set off mid-sentence elements must come in pairs.

### Trap #7: The Possessive Pronoun Apostrophe
An apostrophe appears in a possessive pronoun:

> ✗ *The company released it's annual report.*
> ✓ *The company released its annual report.*

**Defense:** Possessive pronouns (its, theirs, hers, yours, ours, whose) never take apostrophes.

### Trap #8: The Plural Apostrophe
An apostrophe appears in a plural noun that is not possessive:

> ✗ *The student's studied for the exam.*
> ✓ *The students studied for the exam.*

**Defense:** Apostrophes show possession or contraction—never simple plurality.

### Trap #9: The Semicolon with a Dependent Clause
A semicolon appears where one side is not an independent clause:

> ✗ *Although the data were promising; further testing was needed.*
> ✓ *Although the data were promising, further testing was needed.*

**Defense:** Both sides of a semicolon must be independent clauses.

### Trap #10: The "However" Comma Splice
"However" is used with a comma to join two independent clauses:

> ✗ *The data were flawed, however, the conclusions were still valid.*
> ✓ *The data were flawed; however, the conclusions were still valid.*
> ✓ *The data were flawed. However, the conclusions were still valid.*

**Defense:** "However" is a transitional adverb, not a coordinating conjunction. It cannot join two independent clauses with just a comma.

---

## 3.11 Punctuation Rules at a Glance — Quick Reference Table

| Mark | Primary Function | Key Rule | Most Common Error |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Comma (,)** | Separates, sets off, pauses | Every comma needs a grammatical reason | Subject-verb separation; comma splice |
| **Semicolon (;)** | Joins two independent clauses | Both sides must be complete sentences | Using with dependent clauses; missing in complex lists |
| **Colon (:)** | Introduces list, explanation, elaboration | Left side must be a complete sentence | Using after incomplete sentence; confusing with semicolon |
| **Em Dash (—)** | Sets off non-essential elements with emphasis; introduces dramatic explanation | Must match in pairs (mid-sentence); single dash at end | Mixing with commas; unmatched pairs |
| **Apostrophe (')** | Shows possession or contraction | Substitution test for contractions; add 's for singular, s' for plural | Possessive pronouns; plural apostrophes |

---

## 3.12 Advanced Punctuation Concepts

### 3.12.1 The Rhetorical Dash vs. the Grammatical Dash

On the SAT, the em dash sometimes appears in contexts where it serves a rhetorical purpose—to create emphasis, drama, or a sudden shift in tone. In these cases, the dash is not just a grammatical tool but a stylistic choice. The correct answer will be the one that best serves the passage's tone and purpose.

> *The scientist spent decades researching the disease—and finally found a cure.* (The dash creates dramatic emphasis on the discovery.)

Compare:
> *The scientist spent decades researching the disease and finally found a cure.* (The conjunction "and" is grammatically correct but less emphatic.)

On the SAT, if both a dash and a conjunction are grammatically correct, the dash may be preferred if the context calls for emphasis, and the conjunction may be preferred if the context calls for a neutral, matter-of-fact tone.

### 3.12.2 The Colon for Elaboration and Amplification

Beyond introducing lists, a colon can introduce a clause that elaborates on or amplifies the preceding clause. This usage is common in academic and formal writing:

> *The implications are clear: we must invest in renewable energy.*
> *The data tell a consistent story: temperatures are rising, ice is melting, and sea levels are increasing.*

In these cases, the colon functions almost like an equals sign—the right side restates or specifies what the left side introduces.

### 3.12.3 The Semicolon for Contrast

When two independent clauses present contrasting ideas, a semicolon creates a tight connection that highlights the contrast:

> *The wealthy few lived in luxury; the impoverished many struggled to survive.*
> *The theory was elegant; the evidence was lacking.*

This usage is particularly effective because the semicolon's symmetry mirrors the contrast between the two clauses.

### 3.12.4 Parentheses as an Alternative to Commas and Dashes

While the SAT primarily tests commas, semicolons, colons, dashes, and apostrophes, parentheses occasionally appear in answer choices. The key distinction:

- **Commas:** Standard way to set off non-essential elements. Neutral tone.
- **Dashes:** Emphatic way to set off non-essential elements. Draws attention to the enclosed material.
- **Parentheses:** Downplay the enclosed material. Signal that it's truly supplementary.

> *The results (which were unexpected) were published.* (Parentheses downplay the surprise.)
> *The results—which were unexpected—were published.* (Dashes emphasize the surprise.)
> *The results, which were unexpected, were published.* (Commas are neutral.)

On the SAT, if parentheses appear as an option alongside commas and dashes, the choice depends on the context and the degree of emphasis intended.

### 3.12.5 Ellipses and Other Punctuation Marks

Ellipses (...) and exclamation marks (!) rarely appear on the SAT Writing section. The exam focuses on the five marks covered in this chapter. If other punctuation marks appear in answer choices, they are almost certainly incorrect.

---

## 3.13 Punctuation in Context: How the SAT Tests Punctuation Within Passages

On the Digital SAT, punctuation questions are embedded within short passages (25–150 words). The question stem reads: *"Which choice completes the text so that it conforms to the conventions of Standard English?"* This means you must consider not just the sentence containing the blank, but also the surrounding context.

**Key Contextual Considerations:**

1. **Consistency:** If the passage consistently uses the Oxford comma, the correct answer likely does too. If the passage avoids semicolons, a semicolon answer is probably wrong.

2. **Parallelism:** If the passage uses a particular punctuation pattern in similar constructions, the correct answer will maintain that pattern.

3. **Tone:** A formal, academic passage may favor colons and semicolons, while a more conversational passage may favor commas and dashes.

4. **Logical Relationship:** The punctuation should reflect the logical relationship between the ideas it connects. A cause-effect relationship might call for a semicolon + transitional adverb, while a simple list might call for a colon.

5. **Clarity:** When multiple punctuation options are grammatically acceptable, the correct answer is the one that produces the clearest, most unambiguous sentence.

---

## 3.14 Summary: The Five-Step Punctuation Protocol for the SAT

When you encounter any punctuation question on the SAT, apply this protocol:

**Step 1: READ the entire sentence (and the sentences before and after) to understand the context and logical flow.**

**Step 2: IDENTIFY the structural elements** around the punctuation mark. Are there independent clauses? Dependent clauses? Phrases? Lists? Non-essential elements?

**Step 3: NAME the rule being tested.** Is this a comma rule? A semicolon rule? A colon rule? An apostrophe rule? Identifying the rule immediately narrows your options.

**Step 4: APPLY the specific rule** to eliminate wrong answers. Each wrong answer will violate a specific rule or create a structural error.

**Step 5: VERIFY your answer** by reading the sentence with your chosen punctuation. Does it make sense? Is it grammatically correct? Is it the clearest and most concise option?

---

This chapter has provided a comprehensive treatment of every punctuation mark tested on the SAT. Mastery of these rules is not optional—it is essential. Punctuation questions constitute the backbone of the Standard English Conventions domain, and every point you gain here translates directly into a higher score. The rules are specific, the patterns are predictable, and the traps are identifiable. With systematic practice, punctuation questions become among the most reliable points on the entire exam.

---


# Chapter 4: Subject-Verb Agreement and Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement — Rules, Traps, and Edge Cases

---

## 4.0 — Why This Chapter Exists and Why Students Lose Points Here

Subject-verb agreement (SVA) and pronoun-antecedent agreement (PAA) are, by a wide margin, the two most frequently tested grammar rules on the Digital SAT Writing and Language section. They collectively account for a significant portion of the "Form, Structure, and Sense" subdomain within Standard English Conventions. What makes these rules dangerous is not that the underlying logic is difficult — a first-grader can tell you that "The dogs runs" sounds wrong — but that the SAT constructs sentences specifically designed to make you *not see* the true subject or the true antecedent. The test writers are, in essence, magicians who use intervening phrases, inverted word order, and collective nouns as misdirection. This chapter will teach you to see through every trick.

By the end of this chapter, you will be able to:

1. Identify the true subject of any sentence, no matter how many phrases, clauses, or appositives intervene.
2. Apply every major and minor SVA rule, including edge cases involving indefinite pronouns, correlative conjunctions, and notional agreement.
3. Identify the true antecedent of any pronoun and determine whether the reference is clear, ambiguous, or entirely absent.
4. Distinguish between subjective, objective, and possessive pronoun cases and select the correct form.
5. Apply the who/whom test with mechanical precision.
6. Recognize and fix dangling modifiers that masquerade as agreement errors.

---

## 4.1 — The Foundational Principle of Subject-Verb Agreement

### The Core Rule

**A verb must agree in number with its subject.**

- **Singular subject → singular verb**
- **Plural subject → plural verb**

This sounds trivially simple. In practice, the SAT exploits the gap between *what sounds right in casual speech* and *what is correct in formal written English*. Consider:

> *The group of students are waiting in the hall.*

Many native English speakers would not flinch at this sentence in conversation. But in formal writing, the subject is **group** (singular), not **students** (plural). The correct sentence is:

> *The group of students **is** waiting in the hall.*

The prepositional phrase "of students" sits between the subject and the verb, creating a false signal. This is the single most common SVA trap on the SAT, and it is the template for dozens of variations.

### The "Strip the Sentence" Method

When you encounter any SVA question, your first action should be to **strip the sentence** — mentally or on scratch paper — by crossing out every word that is not the subject, the verb, or the essential modifiers of the subject. Specifically, cross out:

1. **All prepositional phrases** that follow the subject (e.g., *of rare coins*, *in the laboratory*, *with the red covers*)
2. **All relative clauses** (e.g., *that was discovered in 1922*, *who are studying for the exam*)
3. **All appositives** (e.g., *a renowned botanist*, *the capital of France*)
4. **All participial phrases** set off by commas (e.g., *running through the park*, *exhausted from the journey*)
5. **All parenthetical expressions** (e.g., *however*, *indeed*, *as a matter of fact*)

After stripping, read the remaining core: **Subject + Verb**. If it sounds wrong when you read it aloud in stripped form, the sentence has an agreement error.

**Example:**

> *The collection of rare manuscripts, some of which date back to the fifteenth century, are housed in the university library.*

Strip it:

> *The collection ~~of rare manuscripts, some of which date back to the fifteenth century,~~ are housed in the university library.*

Core: *The collection are housed.* → This is clearly wrong. The correct verb is **is**.

---

## 4.2 — The "S" Pattern: A Reliable but Imperfect Heuristic

### The General Pattern

In English, there is a surface-level pattern that holds true in the vast majority of cases:

| | Subject | Verb |
|---|---|---|
| **Singular** | Usually does NOT end in "s" | Usually DOES end in "s" |
| **Plural** | Usually DOES end in "s" | Usually does NOT end in "s" |

Examples:

- *The **dog** **runs**.* (singular subject, no "s"; singular verb, has "s")
- *The **dogs** **run**.* (plural subject, has "s"; plural verb, no "s")

This pattern is useful as a quick check, but it has **critical exceptions** that the SAT exploits:

1. **Irregular plural nouns** do not end in "s": *children, people, women, men, mice, geese, data, criteria, phenomena*
2. **Third-person singular verbs** in irregular forms: *is, has, was* (these don't follow the "s" pattern in a predictable way)
3. **Collective nouns** are singular in form but may refer to multiple individuals
4. **Indefinite pronouns** like *everyone, somebody, each* are grammatically singular despite referring to multiple people

### Why the SAT Loves the "S" Trap

The test writers know that students often apply the "s" pattern mechanically. So they construct sentences where the pattern *reverses* the correct answer:

> *The data shows a clear trend.*

A student applying the "s" pattern sees "data" (no "s" → singular?) and "shows" (has "s" → singular verb?) and thinks the sentence is fine. But **data** is the plural of **datum**, and the correct sentence is:

> *The data **show** a clear trend.*

Similarly:

> *The criteria for selection seems unclear.*

**Criteria** is plural (singular: **criterion**). Correct: *The criteria for selection **seem** unclear.*

---

## 4.3 — The Ten Major SVA Rules (With Detailed Explanations and Examples)

### Rule 1: Intervening Phrases Do Not Change the Number of the Subject

**Principle:** Prepositional phrases, relative clauses, appositives, and other modifying phrases that sit between the subject and the verb are **invisible** for agreement purposes. The verb agrees with the subject, not with any noun inside an intervening phrase.

**Common intervening structures:**

| Structure | Example | Trap Noun |
|---|---|---|
| Prepositional phrase | *The box **of chocolates** is on the table.* | chocolates (plural) |
| Relative clause | *The student **who studies hard** succeeds.* | studies (singular verb, but subject is student) |
| Appositive | *The team, **a group of twelve athletes**, is ready.* | athletes (plural) |
| Participial phrase | *The house, **painted white last year**, looks new.* | year (singular, irrelevant) |
| With/along with/as well as | *The teacher, **along with her students**, is attending.* | students (plural) |

**Critical distinction:** Phrases like *along with, as well as, in addition to, together with, accompanied by* do **NOT** create compound subjects. Only **and** creates a compound subject.

- *The CEO, **as well as** the board members, **is** approving the merger.* (Subject = CEO, singular)
- *The CEO **and** the board members **are** approving the merger.* (Compound subject, plural)

**SAT-style example:**

> *The proliferation of social media platforms, driven by the demand for instant communication, have fundamentally altered how people consume news.*

Strip: *The proliferation ~~of social media platforms, driven by the demand for instant communication,~~ have fundamentally altered...*

Core: *The proliferation have...* → Wrong. Correct: *The proliferation **has** fundamentally altered...*

---

### Rule 2: Indefinite Pronouns Are Singular

**Principle:** The following indefinite pronouns are **always grammatically singular** and require singular verbs:

- everyone, everybody, everything
- someone, somebody, something
- anyone, anybody, anything
- no one, nobody, nothing
- each, either, neither (when used as pronouns)
- one, another, much

**Examples:**

- *Everyone **is** welcome to attend.* (NOT: Everyone are welcome)
- *Each of the students **has** a textbook.* (NOT: Each of the students have)
- *Nobody **knows** the answer.* (NOT: Nobody know)
- *Something **is** wrong with the engine.* (NOT: Something are)

**The "Of the..." Trap:**

When an indefinite pronoun is followed by "of the + plural noun," students often match the verb to the plural noun. This is always wrong:

- *Each of the students **has** a textbook.* (subject = each, NOT students)
- *Neither of the options **is** acceptable.* (subject = neither, NOT options)
- *One of the paintings **was** stolen.* (subject = one, NOT paintings)

**Exception — "None":**

"None" is the most debated indefinite pronoun in English grammar. Traditional grammar rules insist that "none" (= not one) is always singular. However, modern usage accepts "none" as plural when it means "not any" of a plural group. **On the SAT, treat "none" as singular** unless the context clearly and unambiguously demands a plural reading, in which case the test will typically avoid the ambiguity altogether.

- *None of the water **is** drinkable.* (water = uncountable → singular, no debate)
- *None of the students **has** finished.* (SAT preference: singular)

---

### Rule 3: Collective Nouns Are Singular (in American English)

**Principle:** Collective nouns — nouns that refer to a group of individuals acting as a unit — are **singular** in American English and require singular verbs.

**Common collective nouns:**

| Noun | Example |
|---|---|
| team | *The team **is** winning.* |
| committee | *The committee **has** reached a decision.* |
| jury | *The jury **was** deliberating.* |
| family | *The family **is** gathering for dinner.* |
| government | *The government **has** passed the law.* |
| audience | *The audience **was** captivated.* |
| class | *The class **is** taking the exam.* |
| crowd | *The crowd **was** growing restless.* |
| staff | *The staff **has** been trained.* |
| majority | *The majority **supports** the proposal.* |
| group | *The group **is** ready to begin.* |
| number | *The number of applicants **has** increased.* |

**The "Individual Members" Exception:**

When the members of a collective are acting individually (not as a unit), British English uses plural verbs. **American English — and the SAT — still treats these as singular.** However, if the sentence explicitly emphasizes individual actions, the SAT may restructure the sentence to avoid ambiguity.

- British: *The team are arguing among themselves.* (individual actions)
- American/SAT: *The team members are arguing among themselves.* (rewritten to avoid the issue)

**Key SAT strategy:** If you see a collective noun as the subject, the verb should be singular. If an answer choice uses a plural verb with a collective noun, eliminate it.

---

### Rule 4: Subjects Joined by "And" Are Plural

**Principle:** When two or more subjects are joined by **and**, the compound subject is **plural** and requires a **plural verb**.

- *Salt **and** pepper **are** on the table.*
- *The teacher **and** the student **are** in the room.*
- *Running **and** swimming **are** excellent forms of exercise.*

**Exception — Compound Nouns Treated as a Single Unit:**

When two nouns joined by "and" form a single, unified concept, the subject is **singular**:

- *Macaroni **and** cheese **is** my favorite dish.* (one dish)
- *The founder **and** CEO **is** stepping down.* (one person)
- *Law **and** order **is** the goal of every government.* (one concept)
- *Bread **and** butter **is** served with every meal.* (one serving item)

**How to tell:** Ask yourself whether the two items are functioning as one thing or two separate things. If you can replace the compound with "it" (singular), it is a single unit. If you can replace it with "they" (plural), it is a compound subject.

- *Macaroni and cheese **is**...* → You can say "**It** is..." → singular
- *The teacher and the student are...* → You can say "**They** are..." → plural

---

### Rule 5: "Either...Or" and "Neither...Nor" — The Proximity Rule

**Principle:** When subjects are joined by **either...or** or **neither...nor**, the verb agrees with the subject **closest to it** (the "proximity rule" or "nearest noun rule").

**Examples:**

- *Either the students or the teacher **is** responsible.* (teacher = singular → is)
- *Either the teacher or the students **are** responsible.* (students = plural → are)
- *Neither the cat nor the dogs **were** inside.* (dogs = plural → were)
- *Neither the dogs nor the cat **was** inside.* (cat = singular → was)

**Why this matters on the SAT:** The test writers will deliberately place the plural noun far from the verb and the singular noun close to it (or vice versa) to create confusion. Always identify the noun immediately before the verb.

**Common error pattern:**

> *Neither the results of the experiment nor the data from the survey **supports** the hypothesis.*

The noun closest to the verb is **data** (plural), so the correct verb is **support**:

> *Neither the results of the experiment nor the data from the survey **support** the hypothesis.*

---

### Rule 6: "Each" and "Every" Are Always Singular

**Principle:** Regardless of what follows, **each** and **every** are singular subjects and require singular verbs.

- *Each student **has** a desk.*
- *Every child **loves** ice cream.*
- *Each of the books **is** signed by the author.*
- *Every one of the cookies **was** eaten.*

**The "Each...and..." Construction:**

When two singular subjects are joined by **each** or **every** before them, the subject is still singular:

- *Each boy and each girl **has** a locker.* (NOT: have)
- *Every man and every woman **is** entitled to vote.* (NOT: are)

**The Postposed "Each":**

When "each" appears after a plural subject, it does **not** change the verb to singular. The verb agrees with the plural subject:

- *The students each **have** a textbook.* (subject = students, plural → have)
- *The books each **weigh** two pounds.* (subject = books, plural → weigh)

This is a subtle but important distinction. Compare:

- *Each of the students **has** a textbook.* (subject = each, singular → has)
- *The students each **have** a textbook.* (subject = students, plural → have)

The position of "each" determines whether it is the subject or merely a modifier.

---

### Rule 7: Inverted Sentences — "There" and "Here"

**Principle:** In sentences beginning with **"there"** or **"here,"** the subject comes **after** the verb. You must identify the true subject to determine agreement.

**Examples:**

- *There **are** many reasons to celebrate.* (subject = reasons, plural → are)
- *There **is** a problem with the system.* (subject = problem, singular → is)
- *Here **are** the documents you requested.* (subject = documents, plural → are)
- *Here **is** the answer to your question.* (subject = answer, singular → is)

**The SAT Trap:**

The test writers will construct long, complex inverted sentences where the subject is buried far from the verb:

> *There is, among the many factors contributing to climate change and the various environmental policies proposed by international organizations, a growing consensus that immediate action is necessary.*

Strip: *There is, among the many factors contributing to climate change and the various environmental policies proposed by international organizations, a growing consensus...*

Subject = **consensus** (singular) → **is** is correct.

**Common error:**

> *There **is** many problems with this approach.*

Subject = **problems** (plural) → Correct: *There **are** many problems with this approach.*

---

### Rule 8: Sentences Beginning with "A Number of" vs. "The Number of"

**Principle:**

- **"A number of"** = plural verb (it means "several" or "many")
- **"The number of"** = singular verb (it refers to the numerical value itself)

**Examples:**

- *A number of students **are** attending the conference.* (several students → plural)
- *The number of applicants **has** increased significantly.* (the numerical value → singular)
- *A number of issues **remain** unresolved.* (several issues → plural)
- *The number of participants **is** limited to fifty.* (the numerical value → singular)

**Memory aid:** "A number of" = "many" = plural. "The number of" = "the count" = singular.

---

### Rule 9: Relative Pronouns as Subjects — "Who," "That," "Which"

**Principle:** When **who**, **that**, or **which** is the subject of a relative clause, the verb agrees with the **antecedent** of the pronoun (the noun the pronoun refers to).

**Examples:**

- *She is one of the researchers who **have** contributed to the field.* (who = researchers, plural → have)
- *He is the only one of the researchers who **has** published this year.* (who = one, singular → has)

**The "One of the...Who" Trap:**

This is one of the most confusing constructions in English grammar. The key is to determine whether "who" refers to "one" or to the plural noun.

**Pattern A: "One of the [plural noun] who..."**

> *She is one of the students who **have** passed the exam.*

Here, "who" refers to **students** (plural), meaning: "Among the students who have passed the exam, she is one." The relative clause modifies "students," not "one."

**Pattern B: "The only one of the [plural noun] who..."**

> *She is the only one of the students who **has** passed the exam.*

Here, "who" refers to **one** (singular), meaning: "Of all the students, she is the only one who has passed." The word "only" restricts the reference to "one."

**The test:** Remove the "one of the" or "the only one of the" and see what the relative clause modifies:

- *The students who **have** passed...* → plural verb
- *The only one who **has** passed...* → singular verb

---

### Rule 10: Titles, Amounts, and Plural-Form Singular Nouns

**Principle:** Certain nouns that appear plural are actually singular:

| Category | Examples | Agreement |
|---|---|---|
| **Titles of works** | *Gone with the Wind* **is** a classic. / *The Canterbury Tales* **is**... | Singular |
| **Amounts (as a unit)** | *Three miles **is** a long way.* / *Ten dollars **is** too much.* / *Five years **is** a long time.* | Singular |
| **Fields of study** | *Mathematics **is** my favorite subject.* / *Economics **is** a challenging field.* / *Politics **is** a dirty business.* | Singular |
| **Diseases** | *Measles **is** contagious.* / *Mumps **is** a childhood illness.* | Singular |
| **Plural-form nouns** | *The news **is** good.* / *Billiards **is** played indoors.* | Singular |

**Exception — Amounts Used Individually:**

When the emphasis is on the individual units rather than the collective amount, a plural verb may be used (though the SAT will typically avoid this ambiguity):

- *The fifty dollars were scattered across the floor.* (individual bills)
- *The three miles were marked with individual mileposts.* (individual miles)

On the SAT, when in doubt, treat amounts as singular.

---

## 4.4 — Advanced SVA Edge Cases

### 4.4.1 — Gerunds as Subjects

When a gerund (an -ing word functioning as a noun) is the subject, it is **always singular**:

- ***Swimming** **is** great exercise.*
- ***Reading** books **improves** vocabulary.*
- ***Having** studied for weeks **was** not enough.*

Even when two gerunds are joined by "and," the subject is usually treated as plural:

- ***Running** and **swimming** **are** both excellent exercises.*

But when two gerunds form a single concept, a singular verb may be used:

- ***Trial** and **error** **is** the best way to learn.* (one concept)

### 4.4.2 — "More Than One" and "Many a"

Despite implying plurality, these expressions take **singular verbs**:

- *More than one student **has** failed the exam.*
- *Many a soldier **has** died for this cause.*
- *More than one option **is** available.*

### 4.4.3 — "Every" and "Many" Before Singular Nouns

When "every" or "many" precedes a singular noun, the subject is singular:

- *Every man and woman **is** entitled to a fair trial.*
- *Many a student **has** struggled with this concept.*

### 4.4.4 — "What" Clauses

When a "what" clause is the subject, it is usually singular:

- *What he needs **is** a good night's sleep.*

But when the predicate after the verb is plural, and the "what" clause clearly refers to plural items, a plural verb may be used:

- *What he found **were** two ancient coins.* (what = the things that → plural)

On the SAT, default to singular for "what" clauses unless the plural reading is unambiguous.

### 4.4.5 — Fractional and Percentage Expressions

When a fraction or percentage is the subject, the verb agrees with the **noun that follows "of"**:

- *Two-thirds of the students **are** present.* (students = plural → are)
- *Two-thirds of the cake **is** gone.* (cake = singular → is)
- *Fifty percent of the water **has** evaporated.* (water = uncountable → has)
- *Fifty percent of the employees **have** resigned.* (employees = plural → have)

### 4.4.6 — "All," "Some," "Any," "Most," "None" + "Of the"

These quantifiers can be singular or plural depending on the noun that follows "of":

- *All of the cake **is** eaten.* (cake = singular)
- *All of the cakes **are** eaten.* (cakes = plural)
- *Some of the information **is** useful.* (information = uncountable)
- *Some of the reports **are** useful.* (reports = plural)
- *Most of the team **is** in agreement.* (team = collective → singular)
- *Most of the players **are** in agreement.* (players = plural)

---

## 4.5 — Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement: The Foundational Principle

### The Core Rule

**A pronoun must agree in number, person, and (where applicable) gender with its antecedent** — the noun it refers to or replaces.

- *The **students** submitted **their** assignments.* (students = plural → their = plural)
- *The **student** submitted **her** assignment.* (student = singular → her = singular)
- ***Each** student submitted **his or her** assignment.* (each = singular → his or her = singular)

### The SAT's Preference: Avoid "Singular They"

In modern conversational English, "they" is widely accepted as a singular pronoun for a person of unspecified gender. **On the SAT, this is generally not accepted.** The test prefers:

- *Each student must bring **his or her** own pencil.*
- *Every participant must submit **his or her** consent form.*

Alternatively, the SAT may restructure the sentence to make the subject plural, allowing "they":

- *All students must bring **their** own pencils.*

**Key SAT strategy:** If you see an answer choice using "they," "their," or "them" to refer to a singular antecedent (like *everyone, each, someone, a student*), eliminate it. The correct answer will either use "his or her" or restructure the sentence.

---

## 4.6 — The Major Pronoun Rules

### Rule 1: Pronoun-Antecedent Number Agreement

The pronoun must match the number of its antecedent:

- *The **team** announced **its** decision.* (team = singular collective → its)
- *The **players** announced **their** decision.* (players = plural → their)
- *Every **student** must bring **his or her** pencil.* (student = singular → his or her)

**The "Shifting Number" Trap:**

> *The company announced that they would be restructuring.*

If "company" is treated as a singular collective noun (American English), the pronoun should be **it**, not **they**. The correct sentence is:

> *The company announced that **it** would be restructuring.*

Or, if the emphasis is on the individual people:

> *The company's executives announced that **they** would be restructuring.*

### Rule 2: Ambiguous Pronoun Reference

**Principle:** Every pronoun must have a **clear, unambiguous antecedent**. If two nouns could logically be the antecedent, the reference is ambiguous and must be fixed.

**Examples:**

> *Maria told Jessica that **she** needed to study more.*

Who is "she"? Maria or Jessica? The sentence is ambiguous. Correct versions:

- *Maria told Jessica, "**I** need to study more."* (direct quote)
- *Maria told Jessica that **Jessica** needed to study more.* (repeat the noun)
- *Maria admitted to Jessica that **she herself** needed to study more.* (reflexive clarifies)

**SAT strategy:** If a pronoun could refer to more than one noun, the correct answer will either (a) repeat the specific noun, (b) restructure the sentence, or (c) use a reflexive pronoun to clarify.

### Rule 3: Pronoun Case

Pronouns change form depending on their function in the sentence. The three cases are:

| Case | Function | Pronouns |
|---|---|---|
| **Subjective (Nominative)** | Subject of a verb | I, he, she, we, they, who, you, it |
| **Objective** | Object of a verb or preposition | me, him, her, us, them, whom, you, it |
| **Possessive** | Shows ownership | my/mine, his, her/hers, our/ours, their/theirs, its, whose, your/yours |

**Common errors:**

| Error | Correction | Explanation |
|---|---|---|
| *Me and my parents ate dinner.* | *My parents and **I** ate dinner.* | Subject position → subjective case |
| *The tourists asked my friends and I for directions.* | *The tourists asked my friends and **me** for directions.* | Object of verb → objective case |
| *Between you and I, this is a bad idea.* | *Between you and **me**, this is a bad idea.* | Object of preposition → objective case |

**The "Remove and Test" Method:**

When a pronoun is part of a compound subject or object, remove the other noun and test the pronoun alone:

- *___ and my parents ate dinner.* → "My parents ate dinner" vs. "Me ate dinner" → "I ate dinner" → **I**
- *The tourists asked my friends and ___.* → "The tourists asked me" (not "The tourists asked I") → **me**
- *Between you and ___* → "Between me" (not "Between I") → **me**

### Rule 4: Who vs. Whom

**Principle:**

- **Who** = subjective case (replaces he/she/they) → used as the subject of a verb
- **Whom** = objective case (replaces him/her/them) → used as the object of a verb or preposition

**The Substitution Test:**

1. Locate the pronoun in the sentence.
2. Try replacing it with **he** (subjective) or **him** (objective).
3. If "he" works, use **who**. If "him" works, use **whom**.

**Examples:**

- *"___ called?"* → "**He** called" → **Who** called?
- *"___ should I call?"* → "I should call **him**" → **Whom** should I call?
- *"The scientist ___ discovered the vaccine..."* → "**He** discovered the vaccine" → **who** discovered the vaccine
- *"The scientist ___ I interviewed..."* → "I interviewed **him**" → **whom** I interviewed

**The "Preposition Signal":**

When the pronoun immediately follows a preposition (to, for, with, about, between, from, etc.), it is almost always **whom**:

- *To **whom** did you speak?*
- *With **whom** are you attending?*
- *From **whom** did you receive the letter?*

**SAT note:** "Whom" appears on the SAT with moderate frequency. If you are unsure, apply the substitution test.

### Rule 5: Relative Pronouns — Who, That, Which

| Pronoun | Used For | Restrictive/Non-Restrictive |
|---|---|---|
| **Who/whom** | People | Both |
| **That** | Things (or people in restrictive clauses) | Restrictive only (no commas) |
| **Which** | Things | Typically non-restrictive (with commas) |

**Examples:**

- *The woman **who** lives next door is a doctor.* (who = subject, refers to people)
- *The book **that** I borrowed was excellent.* (that = refers to things, restrictive)
- *The book, **which** I borrowed yesterday, was excellent.* (which = refers to things, non-restrictive, set off by commas)

**The "That/Which" Rule on the SAT:**

- **Restrictive clause** (essential to identify the noun) → **that** (no commas)
- **Non-restrictive clause** (extra information) → **which** (with commas)

> *The car **that** she bought was red.* (Which car? The one she bought → essential → that, no commas)

> *Her car, **which** she bought last year, is red.* (We already know which car → extra info → which, with commas)

### Rule 6: Possessive Pronouns — No Apostrophes

**Possessive pronouns never take apostrophes:**

| Correct | Incorrect | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| its | it's | belonging to it |
| theirs | their's | belonging to them |
| hers | her's | belonging to her |
| yours | your's | belonging to you |
| ours | our's | belonging to us |
| whose | who's | belonging to whom |

**The Substitution Test for Possessives:**

- *The dog wagged **its** tail.* → Cannot say "it is tail" or "it has tail" → **its** (possessive, no apostrophe)
- ***It's** raining.* → "It is raining" → **it's** (contraction, apostrophe)

---

## 4.7 — Advanced Pronoun Edge Cases

### 4.7.1 — The "One" as a Pronoun

When "one" is used as a pronoun (meaning "any person" or "a person"), it is singular and requires singular pronoun references:

- *One should always do **one's** best.* (NOT: their best)
- *If one works hard, **one** can achieve great things.*

On the SAT, if "one" appears as the antecedent, the pronoun should be "one's" or "his or her" — never "their."

### 4.7.2 — Collective Nouns and Pronoun Agreement

Collective nouns (team, committee, family, government, etc.) are singular in American English and take singular pronouns:

- *The team announced **its** decision.* (NOT: their decision)
- *The committee released **its** report.* (NOT: their report)
- *The family sold **its** house.* (NOT: their house)

**Exception:** If the sentence explicitly refers to the individual members of the collective (not the group as a unit), a plural pronoun may be used. But the SAT will typically restructure to avoid this:

- *The team members argued among **themselves**.* (individual members → plural)
- *The team celebrated **its** victory.* (group as unit → singular)

### 4.7.3 — Reflexive Pronouns

Reflexive pronouns (myself, yourself, himself, herself, itself, ourselves, yourselves, themselves) are used when the subject and object are the same:

- *She hurt **herself**.* (subject = she, object = herself → same person)
- *They prepared **themselves** for the exam.*

**Common error — "Myself" as a substitute for "me" or "I":**

> *My parents and myself went to the store.*

This is incorrect. "Myself" should only be used reflexively (when "I" is the subject and "myself" is the object) or for emphasis:

- *I myself went to the store.* (emphatic)
- *I hurt myself.* (reflexive)
- *My parents and **I** went to the store.* (subject position → I)

### 4.7.4 — Demonstrative Pronouns as Antecedents

When "this," "that," "these," or "those" is used as a pronoun (not a modifier), it must have a clear antecedent:

> *The company lost money. **This** led to layoffs.*

What does "this" refer to? The entire idea of "the company lost money." On the SAT, vague uses of "this" are often corrected by replacing "this" with a more specific noun phrase:

> *The company's financial losses led to layoffs.*

### 4.7.5 — The "Former" and "Latter"

When two items are mentioned, "former" refers to the first and "latter" refers to the second:

- *The report analyzed costs and benefits: the **former** increased while the **latter** decreased.*

The pronoun referring to "former" or "latter" must agree with the number of the noun it represents.

---

## 4.8 — The Intersection of SVA and PVA: Modifier Placement

### Dangling Modifiers

A dangling modifier occurs when the implied subject of an introductory phrase does not match the grammatical subject of the main clause:

> *Hunting for deer, Julian's rifle misfired.*

The introductory phrase "Hunting for deer" implies a subject — someone who is hunting. But the grammatical subject of the main clause is "rifle." A rifle cannot hunt for deer. The sentence dangles.

**Fix options:**

1. Change the subject of the main clause to the person performing the action:
   > *Hunting for deer, **Julian** found that his rifle misfired.*

2. Rewrite the introductory phrase to include its own subject:
   > ***While Julian was** hunting for deer, his rifle misfired.*

**The SAT Test:** Ask "Who or what is performing the action in the introductory phrase?" The answer must be the noun immediately after the comma.

### Misplaced Modifiers

A misplaced modifier is positioned too far from the word it describes, causing it to seem to modify the wrong word:

> *She handed out brownies to children wrapped in foil.*

The phrase "wrapped in foil" should modify "brownies," not "children." Correct:

> *She handed out brownies **wrapped in foil** to children.*

**The SAT Test:** Place the modifier as close as possible to the word it describes.

### Squinting Modifiers

A squinting modifier could refer to either what comes before or after it:

> *Students who study often improve.*

Does this mean "Students who **often** study improve" or "Students who study **often** improve"? The modifier "often" is squinting.

**Fix:** Move the modifier to eliminate ambiguity:

> *Students who **often** study improve.* (frequency of studying)
> *Students who study **often** improve.* (frequency of improving)

---

## 4.9 — Comprehensive SVA/PVA Decision Tree

When you encounter a potential agreement error on the SAT, follow this decision tree:

**Step 1: Identify the subject.**
- Is the sentence inverted (begins with "there" or "here")? → Subject comes after the verb.
- Are there intervening phrases? → Strip them out.
- Is the subject a collective noun? → Treat as singular.
- Is the subject an indefinite pronoun? → Treat as singular.
- Is the subject joined by "and"? → Usually plural (unless it's a single unit).
- Is the subject joined by "or/nor/either...or/neither...nor"? → Verb agrees with the nearest noun.

**Step 2: Identify the verb.**
- Does the verb agree in number with the subject?
- Is the verb in the correct tense?

**Step 3: Identify all pronouns.**
- Does each pronoun have a clear, unambiguous antecedent?
- Does the pronoun agree in number with its antecedent?
- Is the pronoun in the correct case (subjective, objective, possessive)?
- Is "who" vs. "whom" correct?
- Is "that" vs. "which" correct (restrictive vs. non-restrictive)?

**Step 4: Check modifiers.**
- Does the introductory phrase's implied subject match the grammatical subject?
- Are all modifiers placed close to the words they describe?
- Are there any squinting modifiers?

---

## 4.10 — Summary Tables for Quick Reference

### Subject-Verb Agreement Quick Reference

| Situation | Verb Number | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Singular subject | Singular verb | *The dog **runs**.* |
| Plural subject | Plural verb | *The dogs **run**.* |
| Subject + prepositional phrase | Agrees with subject | *The box of chocolates **is**...* |
| Collective noun | Singular verb (AmE) | *The team **is**...* |
| Indefinite pronoun | Singular verb | *Everyone **has**...* |
| "And" compound | Plural verb | *Salt and pepper **are**...* |
| "Or/nor" compound | Agrees with nearest | *Neither the cat nor the dogs **were**...* |
| "Each/every" | Singular verb | *Each student **has**...* |
| "There/here" inversion | Agrees with subject (after verb) | *There **are** many reasons.* |
| "A number of" | Plural verb | *A number of students **are**...* |
| "The number of" | Singular verb | *The number of students **is**...* |
| Amounts as units | Singular verb | *Three miles **is**...* |
| Relative clause (who/that/which) | Agrees with antecedent | *One of the students who **have**...* |
| "The only one of the..." | Singular verb | *The only one who **has**...* |

### Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement Quick Reference

| Situation | Pronoun | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Singular antecedent | Singular pronoun | *The student submitted **his or her** work.* |
| Plural antecedent | Plural pronoun | *The students submitted **their** work.* |
| Collective noun (unit) | Singular pronoun | *The team announced **its** decision.* |
| Collective noun (individuals) | Plural pronoun | *The players argued among **themselves**.* |
| "Everyone/each/someone" | Singular pronoun | *Everyone did **his or her** best.* |
| Subject position | Subjective pronoun | ***She** and I went.* |
| Object position | Objective pronoun | *They asked **her** and **me**.* |
| After preposition | Objective pronoun | *Between you and **me**...* |
| Who (subject) | He/she test | ***Who** called? → **He** called.* |
| Whom (object) | Him/her test | ***Whom** did you call? → I called **him**.* |
| Restrictive clause (things) | That | *The book **that** I read...* |
| Non-restrictive clause (things) | Which (with commas) | *The book, **which** I read,...* |
| Possessive pronoun | No apostrophe | *its, theirs, hers, yours, ours* |

---

## 4.11 — Common SAT Traps: A Comprehensive Catalog

### Trap 1: The Long Intervening Phrase

> *The analysis of the data collected from the surveys conducted in three different cities **are** incomplete.*

**Trap:** "cities" is plural and sits right before "are." Students match the verb to "cities."
**Reality:** Subject = "analysis" (singular). Correct: *The analysis... **is** incomplete.*

### Trap 2: The "Of the" Distraction

> *Each of the recommendations made by the committee **were** implemented.*

**Trap:** "recommendations" is plural and sits right before "were."
**Reality:** Subject = "each" (singular). Correct: *Each of the recommendations... **was** implemented.*

### Trap 3: The Collective Noun Misdirect

> *The jury, after deliberating for three days, have reached their verdict.*

**Trap:** "jury" contains multiple people, so students use plural verbs and pronouns.
**Reality:** "Jury" is a collective noun → singular in American English. Correct: *The jury... **has** reached **its** verdict.*

### Trap 4: The "There Is/There Are" Inversion

> *There is many factors that contribute to climate change.*

**Trap:** "There is" sounds natural in speech.
**Reality:** Subject = "factors" (plural). Correct: *There **are** many factors...*

### Trap 5: The Ambiguous Pronoun

> *When the CEO announced the new policy to the employees, they were confused.*

**Trap:** "They" seems to refer to "employees," but the sentence structure makes it unclear whether "they" refers to the employees or the CEO and employees together.
**Fix:** *The employees were confused when the CEO announced the new policy to them.*

### Trap 6: The "One of the...Who" Confusion

> *She is one of the few people who has ever climbed Mount Everest.*

**Trap:** "few people" is plural, so students use "have."
**Reality:** "Who" refers to "people" (plural), so the verb should be plural. Correct: *She is one of the few people who **have** ever climbed Mount Everest.*

Compare: *She is the only one of the people who **has** ever climbed Mount Everest.* ("Who" refers to "one" → singular.)

### Trap 7: The Singular "They"

> *Someone left their backpack in the classroom.*

**Trap:** This is acceptable in conversational English.
**SAT reality:** "Someone" is singular. Correct: *Someone left **his or her** backpack in the classroom.* OR: *A student left **his or her** backpack in the classroom.*

### Trap 8: The Possessive Pronoun Apostrophe

> *The company released it's annual report.*

**Trap:** Students confuse "it's" (contraction) with "its" (possessive).
**Reality:** Possessive pronouns never take apostrophes. Correct: *The company released **its** annual report.*

### Trap 9: The "Who/Whom" After Preposition

> *The person who I spoke with gave me this information.*

**Trap:** "Who" sounds natural in speech after a preposition.
**SAT reality:** After a preposition, use "whom." Correct: *The person **whom** I spoke with gave me this information.* OR: *The person **with whom** I spoke gave me this information.*

### Trap 10: The Compound Subject with "As Well As"

> *The president, as well as the board members, are attending the meeting.*

**Trap:** "Board members" is plural and sits near the verb.
**Reality:** "As well as" does not create a compound subject. Subject = "president" (singular). Correct: *The president, as well as the board members, **is** attending the meeting.*

---

## 4.12 — The Psychology of Agreement Errors: Why Students Make Mistakes

Understanding *why* you make mistakes is as important as understanding the rules themselves. Here are the cognitive biases that lead to SVA/PVA errors:

### 4.12.1 — Proximity Bias

**What it is:** Your brain tends to match the verb to the noun closest to it, regardless of whether that noun is the true subject.

**Example:** *The box of chocolates are on the table.* Your brain sees "chocolates" (plural) right before "are" and accepts the sentence.

**Fix:** Always strip the sentence before evaluating agreement.

### 4.12.2 — Recency Bias
