**What it is:** In a long sentence, your working memory retains the most recent noun more vividly than the true subject, which may have appeared many words earlier.

**Example:** *The collection of rare manuscripts, some of which date back to the fifteenth century and were discovered in a hidden chamber beneath the monastery, are housed in the library.* By the time you reach "are," you have forgotten that "collection" is the subject.

**Fix:** Physically cross out intervening phrases on scratch paper or mentally bracket them.

### 4.12.3 — Conversational Transfer

**What it is:** You hear certain constructions so frequently in speech that they "sound right" even when they are grammatically wrong.

**Examples:**
- "Everyone are welcome" (common in some dialects)
- "Between you and I" (hypercorrection — people think "I" sounds more formal)
- "There's many problems" (contraction of "there is" before a plural noun)

**Fix:** Trust the rules, not your ear. The SAT tests formal written English, which differs from conversational English in systematic ways.

### 4.12.4 — The "S" Heuristic Overapplication

**What it is:** You learn that singular subjects don't end in "s" and singular verbs do, then apply this rule mechanically without considering exceptions.

**Example:** *The data shows a trend.* You see "data" (no "s") and "shows" (has "s") and think the sentence is fine. But "data" is plural.

**Fix:** Memorize the common irregular plurals: *data, criteria, phenomena, media, bacteria, curricula, memoranda.*

---

## 4.13 — The "Notional Agreement" Concept

In some cases, agreement is determined by **meaning** (notional agreement) rather than **form** (formal agreement). This is rare on the SAT but worth understanding:

- *The majority of the students **are** in favor.* (notional: thinking of the students as individuals)
- *The majority **is** in favor.* (formal: thinking of "majority" as a single entity)

On the SAT, **formal agreement is always preferred.** When in doubt, match the verb to the grammatical form of the subject, not the idea it conveys.

---

## 4.14 — Special Cases and Final Edge Cases

### 4.14.1 — "None"

As noted earlier, "none" can be singular or plural. On the SAT:
- *None of the water **is** drinkable.* (uncountable → singular)
- *None of the students **have** finished.* (The SAT may accept this, but will typically avoid the ambiguity)

### 4.14.2 — "All" and "Any"

These can be singular or plural depending on context:

- *All of the cake **is** eaten.* (cake = singular)
- *All of the cakes **are** eaten.* (cakes = plural)
- *Any of the information **is** useful.* (information = uncountable)
- *Any of the options **are** acceptable.* (options = plural)

### 4.14.3 — "Every" + Singular Noun + "and" + Singular Noun

- *Every man and every woman **is** entitled to vote.* (singular — "every" distributes individually)

### 4.14.4 — "Many a" + Singular Noun

- *Many a student **has** struggled with grammar.* (singular — "many a" + singular noun = singular verb)

### 4.14.5 — "More Than One" + Singular Noun

- *More than one student **has** failed.* (singular — despite implying plurality)

### 4.14.6 — "One of the" + Plural Noun + Relative Clause

- *She is one of the students who **have** passed.* (who = students → plural)
- *She is the only one of the students who **has** passed.* (who = one → singular)

### 4.14.7 — "The" + Adjective = Plural Noun

When "the" is used before an adjective to refer to a group of people (the poor, the rich, the elderly, the young), the subject is **plural**:

- *The poor **are** suffering.* (NOT: The poor is suffering)
- *The elderly **need** better healthcare.* (NOT: The elderly needs)

### 4.14.8 — Acronyms and Abbreviations

- *The MRI **shows** a fracture.* (singular)
- *The MRIs **show** multiple fractures.* (plural)
- *NASA **has** announced...* (singular — one organization)
- *The CEOs **are** meeting.* (plural)

---

## 4.15 — Putting It All Together: The Agreement Checklist

When you encounter any sentence on the SAT, run through this checklist:

**For Subject-Verb Agreement:**
- [ ] Identify the true subject (strip intervening phrases)
- [ ] Determine if the subject is singular or plural
- [ ] Check for collective nouns (singular in AmE)
- [ ] Check for indefinite pronouns (singular)
- [ ] Check for compound subjects (and = plural; or/nor = nearest noun)
- [ ] Check for inverted word order (there/here = subject after verb)
- [ ] Check for "a number of" (plural) vs. "the number of" (singular)
- [ ] Check for amounts as units (singular)
- [ ] Check for relative clauses (who/that/which agrees with antecedent)

**For Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement:**
- [ ] Identify every pronoun and its antecedent
- [ ] Ensure number agreement (singular antecedent → singular pronoun)
- [ ] Ensure clarity (no ambiguous references)
- [ ] Check pronoun case (subjective, objective, possessive)
- [ ] Verify who/whom (he/him test)
- [ ] Verify that/which (restrictive vs. non-restrictive)
- [ ] Check possessive pronouns (no apostrophes)
- [ ] Check reflexive pronouns (subject = object)

**For Modifier Placement:**
- [ ] Check introductory phrases (subject must perform the action)
- [ ] Check end-of-sentence modifiers (should describe the subject)
- [ ] Check for squinting modifiers (ambiguous placement)
- [ ] Ensure modifiers are close to the words they describe

---

This chapter has covered every major and minor rule governing subject-verb agreement and pronoun-antecedent agreement on the SAT. The key to mastery is not memorizing these rules in isolation but applying them systematically through the "strip the sentence" method and the decision tree outlined in Section 4.9. Every time you encounter a sentence, ask: *What is the true subject? What is the true antecedent? Do they agree?* With practice, this process becomes automatic, and agreement errors become impossible to miss.

---

# Chapter 5: Verb Tense, Form, Mood, and Parallel Structure — Consistency Across the Passage

---

## 5.0 — Introduction: Why This Chapter Exists

The SAT Writing and Language section does not test grammar in isolation. It tests whether you can recognize and maintain **consistency** — consistency of tense across a passage, consistency of verb form within a sentence, consistency of mood when the author is being hypothetical or imperative, and consistency of structural form when the author presents two or more items in a series, a comparison, or a correlative pairing. These four domains — **tense, form, mood, and parallel structure** — collectively account for a substantial proportion of Standard English Conventions questions on every Digital SAT. Some questions test only one of these domains in isolation; others test two or three simultaneously, requiring you to evaluate whether a verb is correct in tense, appropriate in form, consistent in mood, and parallel to its neighboring verbs.

This chapter will take you through each domain with exhaustive rigor. By the end, you will be able to identify every type of error the SAT constructs in these areas and understand the reasoning behind every correct answer.

---

## 5.1 — The Nine Basic Tenses of English

Before you can identify an error in tense consistency, you must be able to name every tense and understand what it communicates about time. English has nine standard tenses, organized along three axes (past, present, future) and four aspects (simple, progressive, perfect, perfect progressive).

### 5.1.1 — The Simple Tenses

| Tense | Structure | Example | Time Signal |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Simple Present** | Base verb / base + s | *She walks to school.* / *They walk to school.* | Habitual, general truth |
| **Simple Past** | Past form (regular: -ed; irregular: varies) | *She walked to school.* / *They went to school.* | Completed action in the past |
| **Simple Future** | will + base verb | *She will walk to school.* | Action yet to occur |

**Key SAT Insight:** The simple present is the default tense for passages that describe general truths, scientific facts, or ongoing states. The simple past is the default tense for narratives and historical descriptions. The simple future appears rarely on the SAT outside of passages about predictions or plans.

### 5.1.2 — The Progressive (Continuous) Tenses

| Tense | Structure | Example | Time Signal |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Present Progressive** | am/is/are + present participle (-ing) | *She is walking to school.* | Action happening right now |
| **Past Progressive** | was/were + present participle (-ing) | *She was walking to school.* | Action in progress at a specific past moment |
| **Future Progressive** | will be + present participle (-ing) | *She will be walking to school.* | Action that will be in progress at a future moment |

**Key SAT Insight:** The progressive tenses emphasize the **ongoing, incomplete nature** of an action. On the SAT, a progressive tense is correct when the surrounding context signals that an action was or is in the middle of happening — often paired with an interrupting action in simple tense. For example: *She **was walking** to school when she heard the siren.* The progressive "was walking" sets the scene; the simple past "heard" is the interruption.

### 5.1.3 — The Perfect Tenses

| Tense | Structure | Example | Time Signal |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Present Perfect** | has/have + past participle | *She has walked to school.* | Action started in past, continues to present, or has present relevance |
| **Past Perfect** | had + past participle | *She had walked to school.* | Action completed before another past action |
| **Future Perfect** | will have + past participle | *She will have walked to school.* | Action that will be completed before a future point |

**Key SAT Insight:** The present perfect is one of the most commonly tested tenses on the SAT. The SAT tests it in two specific contexts:

1. **"For" and "since" signals:** When a sentence includes "for" (duration) or "since" (starting point), the present perfect is required. *She has lived in Denver **for** ten years.* / *She has lived in Denver **since** 2014.* Using simple past here (*She lived in Denver for ten years*) changes the meaning — it implies she no longer lives there.

2. **Actions with present relevance:** The present perfect connects a past action to the present moment. *The team has discovered a new species.* (The discovery is relevant now — it affects what we know now.) Contrast with: *The team discovered a new species in 1998.* (Purely historical; the action is over.)

The **past perfect** is tested less frequently but is essential when the passage needs to show that one past event preceded another. *By the time the rescuers arrived, the hikers **had already found** shelter.* Without "had," the sequence is ambiguous.

### 5.1.4 — The Perfect Progressive Tenses

| Tense | Structure | Example | Time Signal |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Present Perfect Progressive** | has/have been + present participle | *She has been walking to school.* | Ongoing action that started in past and continues now |
| **Past Perfect Progressive** | had been + present participle | *She had been walking to school.* | Ongoing action that was happening before another past event |
| **Future Perfect Progressive** | will have been + present participle | *She will have been walking for an hour.* | Ongoing action that will have been happening up to a future point |

**Key SAT Insight:** These tenses appear infrequently on the SAT, but when they do, the key is to check whether the context demands emphasis on the **duration** of an action. *She has been studying for three hours* emphasizes the ongoing duration; *She has studied for three hours* is also correct but focuses on the completion of the study period.

### 5.1.5 — Tense Selection Decision Tree

When you encounter a verb underlined on the SAT, use this decision process:

1. **What is the time frame of the passage?** (Present? Past? Future?) → This eliminates most wrong answers immediately.
2. **Is there a signal word?** (For, since, ago, yesterday, by the time, before, after, etc.) → Signal words often dictate a specific tense.
3. **Is the action completed, ongoing, or completed before another action?** → This determines the aspect (simple, progressive, perfect).
4. **Does the verb agree with the surrounding verbs?** → Consistency is paramount.

---

## 5.2 — Tense Consistency Rules

### 5.2.1 — The Cardinal Rule: Do Not Shift Tense Without Reason

The most fundamental rule of tense usage on the SAT is that **tense must remain consistent within a sentence and across a passage unless there is a logical reason for a shift.** A logical reason includes:

- A time marker that explicitly changes the frame (*In 1998, the team discovered...* shifts from present to past because of "1998")
- A contrast between past and present (*Although the library was built in 1920, it still serves the community.*)
- A flashback or flash-forward in narrative

**Incorrect (unjustified shift):** *The researcher collects data from over 200 participants and found that the results were significant.* (Present "collects" shifts to past "found" with no reason.)

**Correct (justified shift):** *The researcher collected data from over 200 participants and found that the results were significant.* (Both verbs are in simple past — consistent.)

**Also correct (justified shift):** *The researcher **collects** data from over 200 participants and **has found** that the results are significant.* (Present tense for ongoing work; present perfect for results with present relevance.)

### 5.2.2 — The "Time Machine" Principle

Think of a passage as existing in a specific time period. All main verbs should stay in that time period. When the author needs to refer to something before or after that time period, they use the perfect tenses (past perfect for "past of the past," future perfect for "past of the future").

| Passage Time Frame | Referring to Earlier | Referring to Later |
|---|---|---|
| **Present** | Present perfect (has/have + PP) or past perfect if embedded in a past narrative | Simple future (will) or future perfect |
| **Past** | Past perfect (had + PP) | Would + base verb (conditional) or "was/were going to" |
| **Future** | Present perfect or past perfect | Future perfect (will have + PP) |

### 5.2.3 — Tense Consistency in Complex Sentences

When a sentence contains multiple clauses, the tenses do not always have to match — but they must be **logically compatible**. Here are the acceptable patterns:

**Pattern 1: Same tense in both clauses**
*She **studied** hard and **passed** the exam.* (Both simple past — consistent.)

**Pattern 2: Present in one clause, past in another (when justified)**
*The evidence **suggests** that the ancient civilization **collapsed** due to drought.* (Present "suggests" = current scholarly opinion; past "collapsed" = historical event.)

**Pattern 3: Past perfect + simple past (sequence)**
*The train **had already left** when she **arrived** at the station.* (Past perfect "had left" = earlier; simple past "arrived" = later.)

**Pattern 4: Present perfect + simple present (ongoing + general)**
*Scientists **have mapped** the genome, and the data **is** now available to researchers.* (Present perfect = completed action with present relevance; simple present = current state.)

**Pattern 5: "Would" in past-tense narratives**
*He promised he **would finish** the report by Friday.* (In a past-tense narrative, "would" replaces "will.")

**Critical SAT Trap:** The SAT frequently places a "would" option in a present-tense passage or a "will" option in a past-tense passage. Always check the surrounding tense before selecting.

### 5.2.4 — The Subjunctive Mood and Tense Interaction

The subjunctive mood (covered in detail in Section 5.4) creates an exception to normal tense rules. In subjunctive sentences, the verb form appears to be in the "wrong" tense:

*I wish I **were** taller.* (Not "was" — subjunctive "were" signals unreality.)
*If she **were** here, she would agree.* (Not "was" — subjunctive in conditional.)

On the SAT, if you see "was" where the context is hypothetical or contrary to fact, the answer is "were."

---

## 5.3 — Verb Form: Finite vs. Nonfinite Verbs

### 5.3.1 — Finite Verbs

A **finite verb** is a verb that has a specific subject and shows tense. Every independent clause must contain at least one finite verb. A finite verb changes form depending on:

- **Person** (first, second, third)
- **Number** (singular, plural)
- **Tense** (present, past)

Examples:
*She **walks**.* (Third person singular, present)
*They **walk**.* (Third person plural, present)
*She **walked**.* (Past — same form for all persons and numbers)

### 5.3.2 — Nonfinite Verbs

A **nonfinite verb** does not show tense and does not change based on the subject. There are three types:

#### Infinitives (to + base form)
Function: Can act as a noun, adjective, or adverb.
*She wanted **to leave**.* (Infinitive acting as noun — object of "wanted")
*He studied **to pass** the exam.* (Infinitive acting as adverb — purpose)
*The best person **to lead** the team is Maria.* (Infinitive acting as adjective — modifies "person")

**SAT Trap — Split Infinitives:** While not always wrong, the SAT generally avoids split infinitives (placing an adverb between "to" and the verb). *To quickly run* is less preferred than *to run quickly* on formal writing, though this rule is weakening in modern usage.

#### Gerunds (verb + ing, functioning as a noun)
Function: Acts as a subject, object, or complement.
***Swimming** is great exercise.* (Gerund as subject)
*She enjoys **reading**.* (Gerund as direct object)
*His hobby is **painting**.* (Gerund as subject complement)

**SAT Trap — Gerund vs. Infinitive as Object:** Some verbs can take either a gerund or an infinitive, but the meaning changes:

- *She **stopped** **smoking**.* (She quit the habit.)
- *She **stopped** **to smoke**.* (She paused in order to smoke.)

- *He **forgot** **meeting** her.* (He doesn't remember that he met her.)
- *He **forgot** **to meet** her.* (He didn't remember to go meet her — he failed to do it.)

- *She **tried** **eating** less.* (She experimented with it.)
- *She **tried** **to eat** less.* (She made an effort to do it.)

On the SAT, if the meaning of the sentence requires one form over the other, the test will include both as options. Read carefully.

#### Participles (present: -ing; past: -ed or irregular)
Function: Acts as an adjective, modifying a noun or pronoun.

**Present participle (-ing):** Shows active, ongoing action.
*The **burning** building collapsed.* (The building was actively burning.)
*A **fascinating** discovery was made.* (The discovery fascinates people.)

**Past participle (-ed or irregular):** Shows completed action or passive state.
*The **broken** window needs repair.* (The window has been broken — completed action with present result.)
*A **written** agreement is binding.* (The agreement was written — completed action.)

**SAT Trap — Dangling Participles:** A participle at the beginning of a sentence must refer to the subject. (This is covered in the Modifier chapter, but it is a verb form issue as well.)
✗ ***Running** late, the bus was missed by Sarah.* (The bus was not running late.)
✓ ***Running** late, Sarah missed the bus.* (Sarah was running late.)

### 5.3.3 — The SAT's Favorite Verb Form Errors

The SAT tests verb form errors in several predictable patterns:

**Error 1: Using a gerund where an infinitive is required (or vice versa)**
✗ *She enjoys **to swim**.* → ✓ *She enjoys **swimming**.* ("Enjoy" requires a gerund.)
✗ *He decided **going** home.* → ✓ *He decided **to go** home.* ("Decide" requires an infinitive.)

**Common Verbs That Take Gerunds Only:**
enjoy, mind, avoid, consider, suggest, finish, deny, admit, miss, quit, practice, risk, imagine, keep

**Common Verbs That Take Infinitives Only:**
decide, want, need, hope, plan, expect, promise, agree, refuse, learn, afford, seem, appear, claim, manage, offer, pretend, threaten, tend

**Common Verbs That Take Both (with meaning changes):**
remember, forget, stop, try, regret, go on, mean

**Error 2: Using a bare infinitive where a gerund is needed as subject**
✗ *Swim is fun.* → ✓ ***Swimming** is fun.*

**Error 3: Mixing verb forms after modal auxiliaries**
Modal verbs (can, could, will, would, shall, should, may, might, must) are **always** followed by the **base form** of the verb (bare infinitive — no "to," no "-ing," no "-s," no "-ed").
✗ *She can **swimming**.* → ✓ *She can **swim**.*
✗ *He must **to go**.* → ✓ *He must **go**.*
✗ *They should **studying**.* → ✓ *They should **study**.*
✗ *She would **goes**.* → ✓ *She would **go**.*

**Error 4: Using a participle as the main verb**
A sentence cannot have a participle as its only verb. A participle needs a helper (auxiliary verb) to function as a finite verb.
✗ *She **running** to the store.* → ✓ *She **is running** to the store.* (Progressive: auxiliary + present participle)
✗ *The letter **written** yesterday.* → ✓ *The letter **was written** yesterday.* (Passive: auxiliary + past participle)

**Error 5: Faulty sequence of verb forms in compound predicates**
When two verbs share the same subject and are joined by "and," they must be in compatible forms.
✗ *She **enjoys** reading and **to write** fiction.* → ✓ *She **enjoys** reading and **writing** fiction.* (Both gerunds — parallel.)

---

## 5.4 — Mood: The Subjunctive and Beyond

### 5.4.1 — What Is Mood?

Mood is a grammatical category that shows the speaker's attitude toward the action — whether it is factual, a command, a question, or hypothetical/unreal. English has three moods:

1. **Indicative mood:** States facts, asks questions, or expresses opinions. (The vast majority of sentences on the SAT.)
2. **Imperative mood:** Gives commands. (Rare on the SAT.)
3. **Subjunctive mood:** Expresses wishes, hypothetical situations, demands, suggestions, or conditions contrary to fact. (Frequently tested.)

### 5.4.2 — The Present Subjunctive

The present subjunctive uses the **base form of the verb** (no -s, no -ed, no -ing) regardless of the subject. It appears in two contexts:

**Context 1: "That" clauses after verbs of demand, suggestion, or recommendation**

Verbs that trigger the subjunctive: *demand, insist, suggest, recommend, require, ask, urge, propose, advise, mandate, order, request, stipulate, prefer*

Structure: [Verb of demand] + **that** + [subject] + [base verb]

*The committee insisted that the report **be** revised.* (Not "is revised" or "was revised.")
*The doctor recommended that she **take** the medication.* (Not "takes.")
*The rules require that every student **complete** the assignment.* (Not "completes.")

**Key SAT Insight:** The subjunctive looks wrong because it violates subject-verb agreement. "The report **be** revised" — "report" is singular, but "be" has no -s. This is correct because it is subjunctive. The SAT loves to test this by offering "is" or "was" as distractors.

**Context 2: Fixed expressions**

Certain expressions use the present subjunctive as a matter of convention:
- *God **save** the Queen.*
- *Suffice it to **say**...*
- *Come what **may**...*
- *Be that as it **may**...*

### 5.4.3 — The Past Subjunctive

The past subjunctive is used for hypothetical or unreal conditions in the present or future. The key form is **"were"** for all subjects (including singular "I," "he," "she," "it").

**Structure:** If + [subject] + **were** + [complement], [subject] + **would/could/might** + [base verb]

*If I **were** president, I would reform healthcare.* (I am not president — unreal.)
*If she **were** here, she would help us.* (She is not here — unreal.)
*I wish I **were** taller.* (I am not taller — unreal.)

**The SAT's Most Common Subjunctive Test:**
✗ *If I was you, I would accept the offer.* → ✓ *If I **were** you, I would accept the offer.*

The "If I were you" construction is one of the most frequently tested subjunctive patterns on the SAT. Any time the sentence describes a hypothetical or counterfactual condition, "were" is required — even with singular subjects.

### 5.4.4 — The Past Perfect Subjunctive (Unreal Past)

For hypothetical situations in the **past** (things that did not happen), use the **past perfect** in the "if" clause and **would/could/might + have + past participle** in the main clause.

**Structure:** If + [subject] + **had** + [past participle], [subject] + **would/could/might have** + [past participle]

*If I **had known** about the delay, I **would have left** earlier.* (I did not know, so I did not leave earlier.)
*If she **had studied** more, she **might have passed** the exam.* (She did not study more, so she did not pass.)

**SAT Trap:** Mixing the past perfect subjunctive with the past subjunctive:
✗ *If I **would have known**, I **would have helped**.* → ✓ *If I **had known**, I **would have helped**.* (Never use "would" in the "if" clause.)

### 5.4.5 — "Would" Usage Rules

"Would" is one of the most versatile and commonly misused verbs on the SAT. Here are the rules:

**Correct uses of "would":**
1. **Conditional result clause:** *If I had money, I **would buy** a car.*
2. **Future-in-the-past (in past-tense narratives):** *She said she **would call** tomorrow.*
3. **Habitual past action (used to):** *When I was young, I **would visit** my grandmother every summer.*
4. **Polite requests:** ***Would** you mind closing the door?*

**Incorrect uses of "would":**
1. **In the "if" clause of a conditional:** ✗ *If I would have time, I would help.* → ✓ *If I **had** time, I would help.*
2. **As a substitute for simple past:** ✗ *In 1990, the population would reach five billion.* → ✓ *In 1990, the population **reached** five billion.* (Use simple past for completed past actions, not "would.")
3. **For present-tense habitual actions:** ✗ *Every morning, I would go for a run.* → ✓ *Every morning, I **go** for a run.* (Use simple present for current habits.)

---

## 5.5 — Parallel Structure: The Principle of Matching Forms

### 5.5.1 — The Fundamental Rule

**Parallel structure** (also called **parallelism**) is the principle that words, phrases, or clauses that serve the same function in a sentence must be written in the same grammatical form. If two ideas are connected by a coordinating conjunction (FANBOYS), a correlative conjunction (both...and, either...or, neither...nor, not only...but also), or are presented in a list or series, they must match in form.

This is not merely a stylistic preference — on the SAT, faulty parallelism is always an error.

### 5.5.2 — Parallelism in Lists and Series

When three or more items appear in a series, every item must have the same grammatical form.

**All gerunds:**
✗ *She enjoys **hiking**, **swimming**, and **to ride** bikes.* → ✓ *She enjoys **hiking**, **swimming**, and **riding** bikes.*

**All infinitives:**
✗ *He wants to **travel**, **learning** languages, and **meeting** new people.* → ✓ *He wants to **travel**, to **learn** languages, and to **meet** new people.* OR *He wants **to travel**, **to learn** languages, and **to meet** new people.* (The "to" can be distributed to all items or placed only before the first.)

**All nouns:**
✗ *The job requires **experience**, **patience**, and **being** detail-oriented.* → ✓ *The job requires **experience**, **patience**, and **attention to detail**.*

**All adjectives:**
✗ *The movie was **thought-provoking**, **entertaining**, and **had a lot of action**.* → ✓ *The movie was **thought-provoking**, **entertaining**, and **action-packed**.*

**All clauses:**
✗ *The report showed that sales **were rising**, that costs **were stable**, and **profits increased**.* → ✓ *The report showed that sales **were rising**, that costs **were stable**, and that profits **were increasing**.*

### 5.5.3 — Parallelism with Correlative Conjunctions

Correlative conjunctions come in pairs. The structure that follows the first part must match the structure that follows the second part.

| Correlative Pair | Pattern |
|---|---|
| **both...and** | both [X] and [Y] — X and Y must match |
| **either...or** | either [X] or [Y] — X and Y must match |
| **neither...nor** | neither [X] nor [Y] — X and Y must match |
| **not only...but also** | not only [X] but also [Y] — X and Y must match |
| **whether...or** | whether [X] or [Y] — X and Y must match |
| **rather...than** | rather [X] than [Y] — X and Y must match |

**Examples:**

✗ *She **not only** is a talented singer **but also** a skilled dancer.* → ✓ *She is **not only** a talented singer **but also** a skilled dancer.* (Both sides are noun phrases: "a talented singer" / "a skilled dancer.")

✗ *The policy **either** benefits the wealthy **or** it harms the poor.* → ✓ *The policy **either** benefits the wealthy **or** harms the poor.* (Both sides are verb + object phrases: "benefits the wealthy" / "harms the poor.")

✗ *He was **both** admired for his intelligence **and** people respected his honesty.* → ✓ *He was **both** admired for his intelligence **and** respected for his honesty.* (Both sides are past participle + prepositional phrase: "admired for his intelligence" / "respected for his honesty.")

✗ *The movie was **rather** boring **than** it was exciting.* → ✓ *The movie was **rather** boring **than** exciting.* (Both sides are adjectives: "boring" / "exciting.")

**The SAT's Favorite Correlative Trap:** The test frequently places the second correlative word in the wrong position, so the two forms don't match. Always check: what comes immediately after the first correlative word, and what comes immediately after the second? They must be the same part of speech in the same structure.

### 5.5.4 — Parallelism in Comparisons

When comparing two items using "than" or "as," the items being compared must be in the same form.

✗ *Running a marathon is harder than **to swim** a mile.* → ✓ *Running a marathon is harder than **swimming** a mile.* (Both gerunds.)

✗ *She earns more money than **I do**.* → This is actually correct (comparing clause to clause), but also acceptable: *She earns more money than **I**.* (In formal English, "I" is correct because it's the subject of the implied verb "earn.")

✗ *The climate here is similar to **the climate in** Spain.* → ✓ *The climate here is similar to **that of** Spain.* (Comparing climate to climate, not climate to a place.)

✗ *Writing essays is more time-consuming than **multiple-choice questions**.* → ✓ *Writing essays is more time-consuming than **answering multiple-choice questions**.* (Comparing gerund phrase to gerund phrase.)

### 5.5.5 — Parallelism with "And," "But," and "Or"

Even without correlative conjunctions, items joined by "and," "but," or "or" should be parallel when they serve the same function.

✗ *The course is **challenging** and **requires a lot of work**.* → This is actually fine because "challenging" is an adjective and "requires a lot of work" is a verb phrase — they are different functions (subject complement vs. predicate). But if both are meant to describe the course:
✓ *The course is **challenging** and **demanding**.* (Both adjectives.)

✗ *He **opened** the door and **was walking** into the room.* → ✓ *He **opened** the door and **walked** into the room.* (Both simple past — parallel.)

### 5.5.6 — Parallelism in "To" Constructions

When infinitives are listed, the "to" can be placed before the first item only (and understood for the rest) or repeated before each item. Both are correct, but the forms after "to" must match.

✓ *She likes to **run**, **swim**, and **bike**.* (All base verbs after a single "to.")
✓ *She likes **to run**, **to swim**, and **to bike**.* (All full infinitives — also correct, more emphatic.)
✗ *She likes **to run**, **swimming**, and **to bike**.* (Mixed forms — wrong.)

### 5.5.7 — Parallelism with Verbs in Different Voices or Tenses

Sometimes a sentence uses multiple verbs that should be in the same voice or tense for parallelism.

✗ *The architect **designed** the building and it **was built** by her team.* → ✓ *The architect **designed** the building and **oversaw** its construction.* (Both active voice, simple past — parallel.)

However, a shift in voice or tense is acceptable when it reflects a genuine difference in meaning:
✓ *The book **was written** in 1920 and **remains** in print today.* (Past passive for the writing; present active for the current state — justified shift.)

### 5.5.8 — The "Invisible Parallelism" Trap

Some SAT questions hide the parallelism error inside a longer, more complex sentence. The strategy is to identify the conjunction or comparison word and then check what comes before and after it.

**Strategy:**
1. Find the conjunction (and, but, or, than, as, both...and, either...or, not only...but also).
2. Identify the two elements being joined.
3. Check: are they the same grammatical form?
4. If not, find the answer choice that makes them match.

**Example:**
*The new regulations are intended to **reduce pollution**, **improve** air quality, and **the protection** of waterways.*

Step 1: The conjunction is "and" (list).
Step 2: The three items are "reduce pollution," "improve air quality," and "the protection of waterways."
Step 3: "Reduce pollution" = verb + noun. "Improve air quality" = verb + noun. "The protection of waterways" = noun phrase. NOT parallel.
Step 4: Fix: *...to **reduce pollution**, **improve** air quality, and **protect** waterways.* (All verb + noun.)

---

## 5.6 — Advanced Verb Form Issues

### 5.6.1 — The "Have Done" vs. "Did" Distinction

On the SAT, the choice between present perfect and simple past often depends on whether the action has present relevance.

**Present perfect (has/have + past participle):** The action has a connection to the present.
*The company **has released** a new product.* (The product is available now — present relevance.)

**Simple past (past form):** The action is completed and disconnected from the present.
*The company **released** a new product in 2019.* (Historical fact — no present relevance stated.)

**SAT Test:** If the sentence includes a past time marker (*yesterday, in 1998, last week, ago*), the simple past is required. The present perfect cannot be used with a specific past time marker.
✗ *I have seen that movie yesterday.* → ✓ *I **saw** that movie yesterday.*

However, the present perfect CAN be used with "before," "already," "yet," "recently," "just," "still," "so far," "up to now," "since," and "for."

### 5.6.2 — The "Had Had" Construction

The past perfect uses "had" as the auxiliary. When the main verb is also "had," you get "had had." This is grammatically correct and sometimes necessary.

*By the time I arrived, I **had had** three cups of coffee.* (Past perfect of "have" = "had had.")

On the SAT, this construction appears rarely, but when it does, it is often the correct answer when the context requires the past perfect of "have."

### 5.6.3 — Irregular Verb Forms

The SAT occasionally tests irregular past tense and past participle forms. Here are the most commonly tested:

| Base Form | Simple Past | Past Participle |
|---|---|---|
| begin | began | begun |
| break | broke | broken |
| choose | chose | chosen |
| do | did | done |
| drink | drank | drunk |
| drive | drove | driven |
| eat | ate | eaten |
| forget | forgot | forgotten |
| give | gave | given |
| go | went | gone |
| know | knew | known |
| lead | led | led |
| lie (recline) | lay | lain |
| lay (put down) | laid | laid |
| ride | rode | ridden |
| ring | rang | rung |
| rise | rose | risen |
| run | ran | run |
| see | saw | seen |
| shake | shook | shaken |
| speak | spoke | spoken |
| swim | swam | swum |
| take | took | taken |
| tear | tore | torn |
| throw | threw | thrown |
| wear | wore | worn |
| write | wrote | written |

**Key SAT Distinction: "Lay" vs. "Lie"**

This is one of the most commonly confused verb pairs on the SAT:

- **Lay** (transitive — requires an object): *lay, laid, laid*
  *She **laid** the book on the table.* (Past tense of "lay" — she put the book down.)
  *She has **laid** the book on the table.* (Past participle.)

- **Lie** (intransitive — no object): *lie, lay, lain*
  *She **lay** on the couch for an hour.* (Past tense of "lie" — she reclined. Note: "lay" is both the past tense of "lie" AND the present tense of "lay" — this is why it's confusing.)
  *She has **lain** on the couch for an hour.* (Past participle.)

**SAT Trap:** *He **layed** down on the bed.* → ✓ *He **lay** down on the bed.* (Past tense of "lie" is "lay," not "layed.")

### 5.6.4 — Passive Voice vs. Active Voice (Verb Form Implications)

The passive voice uses a form of "be" + past participle. While the SAT does not always penalize the passive voice, it does test whether the verb form is correct within the passive construction.

**Active:** *The team **discovered** a new species.* (Simple past, active)
**Passive:** *A new species **was discovered** by the team.* (Simple past, passive: "was" + past participle)

**Active:** *The committee **has approved** the plan.* (Present perfect, active)
**Passive:** *The plan **has been approved** by the committee.* (Present perfect, passive: "has been" + past participle)

**Active:** *The company **will release** the product next month.* (Simple future, active)
**Passive:** *The product **will be released** next month.* (Simple future, passive: "will be" + past participle)

**When passive is preferred on the SAT:**
- When the agent (the doer) is unknown or unimportant: *The building **was constructed** in 1920.* (We don't know who built it.)
- When the focus should be on the receiver of the action: *The law **was passed** unanimously.* (The law is the focus, not who passed it.)

**When active is preferred:**
- When the agent is known and important: ✗ *The theory was proposed by Einstein.* → ✓ *Einstein **proposed** the theory.* (More direct and concise.)

---

## 5.7 — Comprehensive Parallel Structure Patterns

### 5.7.1 — Pattern Matching Reference Table

| If the first item is... | The second item must be... | Example |
|---|---|---|
| a noun | a noun | *She is a **writer** and a **musician**.* |
| an adjective | an adjective | *The movie was **long** and **boring**.* |
| a gerund | a gerund | *He enjoys **running** and **cycling**.* |
| an infinitive | an infinitive | *She wants to **sing** and to **dance**.* |
| a past participle | a past participle | *The building was **designed** and **built** in 1920.* |
| a present participle | a present participle | *The dog was **barking** and **running** around.* |
| a prepositional phrase | a prepositional phrase | *She walked **through the park** and **along the river**.* |
| a clause | a clause | *He said that he **would come** and that he **would bring** food.* |
| an adverb | an adverb | *She spoke **clearly** and **confidently**.* |

### 5.7.2 — The "Mixed Parallelism" Error

The SAT's most sophisticated parallelism errors mix two different structures. Here are the most common mixed patterns:

**Mixing a gerund and an infinitive:**
✗ *The job involves **writing** reports and **to manage** the budget.* → ✓ *...**writing** reports and **managing** the budget.*

**Mixing a noun and a clause:**
✗ *He mentioned **his experience** and **that he had traveled** abroad.* → ✓ *...**his experience** and **his travels** abroad.* (Both noun phrases) OR *...**that he had experience** and **that he had traveled** abroad.* (Both clauses)

**Mixing an adjective and a clause:**
✗ *She is **intelligent** and **who works** hard.* → ✓ *She is **intelligent** and **hardworking**.* (Both adjectives)

**Mixing active and passive voice:**
✗ *The architect **designed** the house and **it was built** by contractors.* → ✓ *The architect **designed** the house and **oversaw** its construction.* (Both active)

### 5.7.3 — Parallelism Across Sentences

While most parallelism errors occur within a single sentence, the SAT occasionally tests parallelism across sentences — particularly in passages where the author uses a repeated structure for rhetorical effect. If one sentence in a series uses a gerund, the others should too.

**Example:**
*The program offers several benefits. **Reducing** costs is one. **Improving** efficiency is another. And to increase employee satisfaction is a third.* → The third sentence breaks the pattern. ✓ *And **increasing** employee satisfaction is a third.*

---

## 5.8 — Strategy Summary: The Verb Form and Parallelism Checklist

When you encounter an underlined verb or a series of items on the SAT, run through this checklist:

### For Verb Tense:
- [ ] What is the established time frame of the passage?
- [ ] Is there a signal word (for, since, ago, yesterday, by the time)?
- [ ] Does the verb match the surrounding verbs in tense?
- [ ] If the action happened before another past action, is the past perfect used?
- [ ] If the action started in the past and continues now, is the present perfect used?
- [ ] Is "would" used correctly (in the result clause, not the "if" clause)?

### For Verb Form:
- [ ] Is the verb finite (has a subject and shows tense) where a finite verb is needed?
- [ ] After a modal (can, could, will, would, shall, should, may, might, must), is the base form used?
- [ ] After verbs that require gerunds (enjoy, avoid, etc.), is a gerund used?
- [ ] After verbs that require infinitives (decide, want, etc.), is an infinitive used?
- [ ] Is the participle (-ing or -ed) used correctly as an adjective, not as the main verb?
- [ ] Is the irregular verb form correct?

### For Mood:
- [ ] Is "were" used (not "was") for hypothetical/counterfactual conditions?
- [ ] Is the subjunctive used after verbs of demand/suggestion (base form, no -s)?
- [ ] Is "would" avoided in "if" clauses?

### For Parallel Structure:
- [ ] Are items in a list all the same grammatical form?
- [ ] After correlative conjunctions (both...and, either...or, not only...but also), do the two sides match?
- [ ] In comparisons with "than" or "as," are the items being compared in the same form?
- [ ] When verbs are joined by "and," are they in the same form (same tense, same voice, same type)?

---

## 5.9 — Common SAT Distractors in This Domain

Understanding the wrong answers is just as important as understanding the right ones. Here are the most common distractor patterns the SAT uses in verb tense, form, mood, and parallelism questions:

**Distractor 1: The Unjustified Tense Shift**
The wrong answer shifts tense when the context does not justify it. If the passage is in the past tense and the underlined verb is in the past, an option in the present tense is almost certainly wrong.

**Distractor 2: The "Would" in the "If" Clause**
Whenever you see a conditional sentence, check if "would" appears in the "if" clause. It never should (except in polite requests: *If you would please take a seat...*).

**Distractor 3: The "Was" Instead of "Were"**
In hypothetical sentences, "was" is always wrong. The correct form is "were" for all subjects.

**Distractor 4: The Mixed Parallel Form**
The wrong answer changes the grammatical form partway through a series. If the first two items are gerunds and the third is an infinitive, the third is wrong.

**Distractor 5: The "To" Placement Error**
In a series of infinitives, the "to" can be omitted after the first item, but the verb forms must all be base verbs. An option that changes one to a gerund is wrong.

**Distractor 6: The Modal + Wrong Form**
After a modal verb, any form other than the base form is wrong. "Can goes," "must to go," "should going" — all incorrect.

**Distractor 7: The Present Perfect with Past Time Marker**
"Have seen yesterday" is always wrong. Present perfect cannot be used with a specific past time.

**Distractor 8: The Passive Voice Distractor**
Sometimes the SAT offers a passive construction as a distractor when the active voice is more concise and direct. If both are grammatically correct, the active voice is preferred.

---

## 5.10 — Putting It All Together: Passage-Level Analysis

On the actual SAT, you will not encounter these rules in isolation. A single question might test tense consistency AND verb form AND parallelism simultaneously. Here is how to approach such questions:

**Step 1: Read the entire sentence containing the underlined portion.**
Do not just look at the underlined words. Context is everything.

**Step 2: Identify what the question is testing.**
Is it a verb? Check tense, form, and mood. Is it a series or comparison? Check parallelism. Is it a conditional? Check subjunctive.

**Step 3: Check the surrounding verbs.**
If the passage is in the past tense, the underlined verb should probably be in the past tense too (unless there is a reason to shift).

**Step 4: Apply the specific rule.**
Use the checklists above to evaluate each answer choice.

**Step 5: Read the sentence with your chosen answer.**
Does it sound right? Does it maintain consistency with the rest of the passage? If something feels off, re-evaluate.

**Step 6: Eliminate wrong answers.**
Cross out choices that violate the rules you've identified. The correct answer will be the one that satisfies all requirements — tense consistency, correct verb form, appropriate mood, and parallel structure.

---

This chapter has provided the comprehensive theoretical foundation for understanding verb tense, form, mood, and parallel structure on the SAT. Every rule, every exception, every trap, and every strategy has been laid out. The key to mastery is not memorization alone but **application** — recognizing these patterns instantly when you encounter them on test day. The grammar rules must become automatic, so that your mental energy can be spent on the more complex rhetorical and reading comprehension questions that also demand your attention.

---


# Chapter 6: Modifier Placement — Dangling, Misplaced, and Squinting Modifiers with Correction Frameworks

## 6.1 — The Architecture of Modifiers: Foundational Principles

Before we dissect the three categories of modifier errors that appear on the SAT, we must first establish a rigorous understanding of what modifiers are, how they function within sentences, and why their placement is not merely a stylistic preference but a grammatical necessity.

### What Is a Modifier?

A modifier is any word, phrase, or clause that describes, clarifies, qualifies, or limits another word in a sentence. Modifiers can be single words (adjectives, adverbs), phrases (participial phrases, prepositional phrases, infinitive phrases), or clauses (relative clauses, adverbial clauses). Their fundamental purpose is to add detail — but that detail must be attached to the correct target. When it is not, meaning collapses into ambiguity or absurdity.

**Example of a modifier functioning correctly:**
> *Exhausted from the marathon, Sarah collapsed on the couch.*

Here, the participial phrase "Exhausted from the marathon" correctly modifies "Sarah," the subject of the sentence. Sarah is the one who was exhausted. The modifier and its target are in proper alignment.

**Example of a modifier functioning incorrectly:**
> *Exhausted from the marathon, the couch looked inviting.*

Here, the participial phrase "Exhausted from the marathon" appears to modify "the couch," because the couch is the subject of the sentence. This is absurd — a couch cannot be exhausted. This is a **dangling modifier**.

### The Proximity Principle

English relies heavily on **proximity** — the physical closeness between a modifier and the word it modifies — to convey meaning. Unlike Latin or German, which use case endings to show grammatical relationships, English depends on word order. This means that **where** you place a modifier is often as important as **what** the modifier says.

The general rule is simple: **Place the modifier as close as possible to the word it modifies.**

When a modifier is separated from its intended target by intervening words, clauses, or phrases, the reader may attach the modifier to the wrong word, leading to confusion, ambiguity, or unintended humor.

### The Three Categories of Modifier Errors

The SAT tests three distinct types of modifier placement errors:

1. **Dangling Modifiers** — The modifier has no logical target in the sentence at all.
2. **Misplaced Modifiers** — The modifier has a logical target, but it is placed too far from it, causing it to appear to modify the wrong word.
3. **Squinting Modifiers** — The modifier is positioned ambiguously between two possible targets, and the reader cannot determine which one is intended.

Each type requires a different diagnostic approach and a different correction strategy. We will examine each in exhaustive detail.

---

## 6.2 — Dangling Modifiers: The Absent Target

### Definition

A dangling modifier occurs when the word that the modifier is *intended* to describe is **absent from the sentence entirely**. The modifier dangles because there is no grammatical element in the sentence for it to attach to logically.

This most commonly happens when a sentence begins with an introductory participial phrase (ending in *-ing* or *-ed*) or an infinitive phrase, and the subject of the main clause is not the person or thing performing the action described in that introductory phrase.

### The Diagnostic Test: "Who/What Performs the Action?"

To detect a dangling modifier, follow this procedure:

1. **Identify the introductory modifying phrase** (usually at the beginning of the sentence).
2. **Ask: "Who or what is performing the action described in this phrase?"**
3. **Check: Is that person or thing the grammatical subject of the main clause?**
4. If the answer is **no** — the modifier dangles.

### Detailed Examples and Analysis

**Example 1:**
> *Walking through the forest, the trees were beautiful.*

- **Introductory phrase:** "Walking through the forest"
- **Action:** Walking
- **Who is walking?** Presumably a person.
- **Subject of main clause:** "the trees"
- **Do trees walk?** No.
- **Diagnosis:** Dangling modifier. The person who was walking is not present in the sentence.
- **Correction:** *Walking through the forest, **I** thought the trees were beautiful.* OR *As **I** walked through the forest, the trees were beautiful.*

**Example 2:**
> *Having finished the assignment, the TV was turned on.*

- **Introductory phrase:** "Having finished the assignment"
- **Action:** Finishing the assignment
- **Who finished?** A person (a student, presumably).
- **Subject of main clause:** "the TV"
- **Does a TV finish assignments?** No.
- **Diagnosis:** Dangling modifier.
- **Correction:** *Having finished the assignment, **she** turned on the TV.*

**Example 3:**
> *To improve her grades, studying was necessary.*

- **Introductory phrase:** "To improve her grades"
- **Action:** Improving
- **Who needs to improve?** "Her" — a specific person.
- **Subject of main clause:** "studying" (a gerund)
- **Can studying improve grades?** This is conceptually possible, but the phrase "her grades" implies a specific person who must do the studying, and "studying" as a gerund is not a person.
- **Diagnosis:** Dangling modifier.
- **Correction:** *To improve her grades, **she** needed to study.*

**Example 4:**
> *After reading the novel, the movie seemed disappointing.*

- **Introductory phrase:** "After reading the novel"
- **Action:** Reading
- **Who read the novel?** A person (the speaker).
- **Subject of main clause:** "the movie"
- **Does a movie read novels?** No.
- **Diagnosis:** Dangling modifier.
- **Correction:** *After reading the novel, **I** found the movie disappointing.*

### Common Patterns That Produce Dangling Modifiers

Understanding *why* dangling modifiers occur helps you avoid them and identify them quickly on the test.

**Pattern 1: Passive Voice Concealing the Agent**

When the main clause uses passive voice, the agent (the doer of the action) is often omitted, leaving the introductory modifier with no one to attach to.

> *After being trained for months, the competition was entered by the dog.*

Here, "the dog" is in a prepositional phrase ("by the dog"), not in the subject position. The subject is "the competition," which cannot be trained.

**Correction:** *After being trained for months, the dog entered the competition.*

**Pattern 2: Gerund or Abstract Noun as Subject**

When the subject of the main clause is a gerund (an *-ing* word functioning as a noun) or an abstract noun (like "success," "results," "data"), the introductory participial phrase may dangle because these nouns cannot perform human actions.

> *By studying diligently, success was achieved.*

"Success" cannot study.

**Correction:** *By studying diligently, **the students** achieved success.*

**Pattern 3: "It" or "There" Constructions**

Sentences beginning with "It is" or "There is/are" can create dangling modifiers because the true agent is buried or absent.

> *It being a rainy day, the picnic was cancelled.*

While this is technically grammatical (an absolute construction), the SAT may test a variant where the modifier clearly refers to people who are not the subject.

> *Being a rainy day, the decision was made to stay indoors.*

"Being a rainy day" modifies... what? The decision? No. The people who decided? They're not in the sentence.

**Correction:** *Because **it was** a rainy day, **we** decided to stay indoors.*

### The Correction Framework for Dangling Modifiers

There are **two primary strategies** to fix a dangling modifier:

**Strategy 1: Change the Subject of the Main Clause**

Replace the subject of the main clause with the person or thing that performs the action in the introductory phrase.

| Dangling Sentence | Correction |
|---|---|
| *Running late, the bus was missed.* | *Running late, **I** missed the bus.* |
| *Having been delayed, the meeting started late.* | *Having been delayed, **the committee** started the meeting late.* |

**Strategy 2: Rewrite the Introductory Phrase as a Dependent Clause**

Convert the participial phrase into an adverbial clause with its own subject and verb.

| Dangling Sentence | Correction |
|---|---|
| *Exhausted from the journey, the bed looked comfortable.* | *Because **I was** exhausted from the journey, the bed looked comfortable.* |
| *To succeed in college, discipline is required.* | *If **you want** to succeed in college, discipline is required.* |

Both strategies are equally valid on the SAT. The correct answer choice will use whichever approach produces the clearest, most concise sentence.

### Special Case: Acceptable "Dangling" Modifiers (Sentence Modifiers)

Some introductory phrases have become so conventional in English that they function as **sentence modifiers** (also called **free modifiers** or **absolute constructions**) and do not need to attach to a specific subject. These are **not** considered errors.

Examples of acceptable sentence modifiers:
- *Generally speaking, the economy is improving.*
- *To be honest, I disagree with the policy.*
- *Interestingly, the results contradicted the hypothesis.*
- *In conclusion, the data support the theory.*

These phrases modify the **entire sentence or the speaker's attitude**, not a specific noun. The SAT does **not** test these as errors. However, if a participial phrase clearly implies a specific agent that is absent, it **is** an error.

**Key Distinction:**
- *Frankly, the plan is flawed.* → "Frankly" modifies the speaker's attitude. ✅ Acceptable.
- *Reviewing the plan, several errors were found.* → Someone reviewed the plan, but who? ❌ Dangling.

---

## 6.3 — Misplaced Modifiers: The Distant Target

### Definition

A misplaced modifier occurs when the word the modifier *intended* to describe **is present in the sentence**, but the modifier is placed too far from it, causing the modifier to appear to describe a different word — usually the nearest noun.

Unlike a dangling modifier (where the target is absent), a misplaced modifier has a clear target that is simply in the wrong position relative to the modifier.

### The Diagnostic Test: "What Should This Modifier Describe?"

To detect a misplaced modifier:

1. **Identify the modifier** (a word, phrase, or clause that describes something).
2. **Ask: "What word is this modifier *intended* to describe?"**
3. **Check: "What word does the modifier *actually* appear to describe based on its position?"**
4. If the two do not match — the modifier is misplaced.

### Detailed Examples and Analysis

**Example 1:**
> *She handed out brownies to children wrapped in foil.*

- **Modifier:** "wrapped in foil"
- **Intended target:** "brownies" (the brownies were wrapped in foil)
- **Actual nearest noun:** "children"
- **What the sentence literally says:** The children were wrapped in foil.
- **Diagnosis:** Misplaced modifier.
- **Correction:** *She handed out brownies **wrapped in foil** to children.*

**Example 2:**
> *The professor only teaches graduate students on Thursdays.*

- **Modifier:** "only"
- **Intended target:** This is ambiguous. Does it mean:
  - (a) The professor teaches **only** graduate students (not undergraduates) on Thursdays?
  - (b) The professor teaches graduate students **only** on Thursdays (not other days)?
- **Actual placement:** "only" is between "teaches" and "graduate students," suggesting interpretation (a). But the intended meaning might be (b).
- **Diagnosis:** Misplaced modifier (specifically, a misplaced limiting modifier).
- **Correction for (a):** *The professor teaches **only** graduate students on Thursdays.*
- **Correction for (b):** *The professor teaches graduate students **only** on Thursdays.*

**Example 3:**
> *The man walked into the store with a limp.*

- **Modifier:** "with a limp"
- **Intended target:** "The man" (the man has a limp)
- **Actual nearest noun:** "the store"
- **What the sentence literally says:** The store has a limp. (Absurd.)
- **Diagnosis:** Misplaced modifier.
- **Correction:** *The man **with a limp** walked into the store.* OR *The man **who had a limp** walked into the store.*

**Example 4:**
> *I almost drove my kids to school every day.*

- **Modifier:** "almost"
- **Intended target:** "every day" (I drove them on nearly every day)
- **Actual placement:** "almost" is before "drove," suggesting I *almost* drove them (but didn't actually drive them — perhaps I almost got in the car but didn't).
- **Diagnosis:** Misplaced modifier.
- **Correction:** *I drove my kids to school **almost** every day.*

### The "Only" Problem: Limiting Modifiers

The word **"only"** is one of the most frequently misplaced modifiers in English, and the SAT is aware of this. "Only" is a **limiting modifier** — it restricts or limits the word it modifies. Its meaning changes dramatically based on its position:

| Sentence | Meaning |
|---|---|
| *Only **I** love you.* | No one else loves you. |
| *I **only** love you.* | I love you and nothing else. |
| *I love **only** you.* | I love no one else. |
| *I love you **only**.* | I love you and nothing/no one else. |

In each case, "only" modifies the word **immediately before or after it** (depending on context). The SAT may test whether the placement of "only" (or similar limiting modifiers like *just, nearly, almost, even, merely, exactly*) matches the intended meaning.

**SAT-Style Test:**
> *The committee only approved three proposals.*

If the intended meaning is that the committee approved **only three** (not five, not ten), then "only" should be placed before "three":
> *The committee approved **only** three proposals.*

If the intended meaning is that the committee's **sole action** was approving (not rejecting, not tabling), then the placement is acceptable.

### Single-Word Modifiers: Adverbs and Adjectives

Single-word modifiers — especially adverbs — are frequently misplaced. The most commonly tested adverbs on the SAT include:

- **only**
- **almost**
- **nearly**
- **just**
- **even**
- **merely**
- **exactly**
- **hardly**
- **scarcely**

**Rule:** Place the modifying adverb **as close as possible** to the word it modifies.

> *She **nearly** scored a perfect 1600 on the SAT.* (She came close to a perfect score.)
> *She scored **nearly** a perfect 1600 on the SAT.* (Her score was close to 1600 — perhaps 1580.)

These two sentences have **different meanings** based on adverb placement. The SAT may test this distinction.

### Phrasal Modifiers: Prepositional and Participial Phrases

Multi-word modifiers (phrases) are also subject to misplacement.

**Prepositional Phrases as Modifiers:**
> *The book on the shelf with the red cover is mine.*

- **Modifier:** "with the red cover"
- **Intended target:** "The book" (the book has a red cover)
- **Actual nearest noun:** "the shelf"
- **What the sentence literally says:** The shelf has a red cover.
- **Diagnosis:** Misplaced modifier.
- **Correction:** *The book **with the red cover** on the shelf is mine.*

**Participial Phrases as Modifiers:**
> *Covered in dust, Maria cleaned the attic.*

Wait — this one is tricky. "Covered in dust" modifies... the attic (which was covered in dust) or Maria (who was covered in dust)? Context suggests the attic, but proximity suggests Maria.

- **Correction:** *Maria cleaned the attic, **which was covered in dust**.* OR *The attic, **covered in dust**, was cleaned by Maria.*

### The Correction Framework for Misplaced Modifiers

**The single fix is always: Move the modifier closer to its intended target.**

| Misplaced Sentence | Correction |
|---|---|
| *The dog chased the ball wearing a collar.* | *The dog **wearing a collar** chased the ball.* |
| *I read a book about dinosaurs in the library.* | *I read a book **about dinosaurs** in the library.* (if the book is about dinosaurs) OR *In the library, I read a book about dinosaurs.* (if the reading happened in the library) |
| *The students were praised by the teacher who studied hard.* | *The students **who studied hard** were praised by the teacher.* |

### End-of-Sentence Modifiers: A Special Note

The SAT may test whether a modifier at the **end of a sentence** is correctly placed. A modifier at the end of a sentence modifies the **subject** of the sentence, even if it is far from the subject in terms of linear distance.

> *The magician walked across the stage, dazzling the crowd.*

Here, "dazzling the crowd" modifies "the magician" — the magician is the one doing the dazzling. This is **correct** because the magician is the subject. The modifier is at the end, but it correctly refers back to the subject.

However, if the sentence were:
> *The magician walked across the crowd, dazzling the stage.*

This would be incorrect because "dazzling the stage" would modify "the crowd" (the nearest noun), and the crowd is not dazzling the stage. The magician is.

**Key Principle:** End-of-sentence modifiers modify the **subject** of the sentence, not the nearest noun. If the nearest noun is not the subject, the modifier is misplaced.

---

## 6.4 — Squinting Modifiers: The Ambiguous Target

### Definition

A squinting modifier (also called a **two-way modifier**) is positioned in the sentence such that it could logically modify **either the word before it or the word after it**, creating ambiguity. The reader cannot determine which modification is intended.

The term "squinting" is metaphorical — the modifier appears to "squint" in both directions simultaneously, looking at two possible targets.

### The Diagnostic Test: "Could This Modifier Describe Two Different Things?"

To detect a squinting modifier:

1. **Identify the modifier.**
2. **Ask: "Could this modifier logically describe the word BEFORE it?"**
3. **Ask: "Could this modifier logically describe the word AFTER it?"**
4. If the answer to **both** questions is "yes" — the modifier is squinting.

### Detailed Examples and Analysis

**Example 1:**
> *Students who study often improve.*

- **Modifier:** "often"
- **Could "often" modify "study"?** Yes — students who **often** study (study frequently) improve.
- **Could "often" modify "improve"?** Yes — students who study **often** improve (improve frequently).
- **Diagnosis:** Squinting modifier. The sentence has two equally valid readings.
- **Correction for first meaning:** *Students who **often** study improve.*
- **Correction for second meaning:** *Students who study improve **often**.*

**Example 2:**
> *The professor said yesterday that the exam would be cancelled.*

- **Modifier:** "yesterday"
- **Could "yesterday" modify "said"?** Yes — the professor **yesterday** said something.
- **Could "yesterday" modify "would be cancelled"?** Yes — the exam would be cancelled **yesterday**.
- **Diagnosis:** Squinting modifier.
- **Correction for first meaning:** *Yesterday, the professor said that the exam would be cancelled.*
- **Correction for second meaning:** *The professor said that the exam would be cancelled **yesterday**.*

**Example 3:**
> *People who drive slowly are dangerous.*

- **Modifier:** "slowly"
- **Wait — "slowly" can only modify "drive."** This is not a squinting modifier. It is correctly placed. (This is a distractor to test your precision.)
- **Diagnosis:** Not a squinting modifier. "Slowly" clearly modifies "drive."

**Example 4:**
> *She will explain the plan to the team that she developed.*

- **Modifier:** "that she developed"
- **Could "that she developed" modify "the plan"?** Yes — she developed the plan.
- **Could "that she developed" modify "the team"?** Yes — she developed the team (as in, built the team over time).
- **Diagnosis:** Squinting modifier (technically a squinting relative clause).
- **Correction:** *She will explain **the plan that she developed** to the team.* OR *She will explain the plan to the team **that she developed**.* (The second correction requires context to be clear.)

### Why Squinting Modifiers Are SAT Favorites

Squinting modifiers are particularly attractive to test-makers because they create a specific type of ambiguity that requires careful reading. The SAT tests whether you can:

1. Recognize that a sentence has **two possible meanings**.
2. Identify the **specific word** causing the ambiguity.
3. Select the answer choice that **eliminates the ambiguity** through repositioning.

On the SAT, you will rarely be asked to identify the error type by name. Instead, you will see answer choices that offer different placements for the same modifier, and you must select the placement that produces a clear, unambiguous sentence.

### The Correction Framework for Squinting Modifiers

**Move the modifier so that it can only modify one word — the intended one.**

| Squinting Sentence | Correction |
|---|---|
| *Students who study often improve.* | *Students who **often** study improve.* OR *Students who study improve **often**.* |
| *The professor said yesterday that the exam would be cancelled.* | *Yesterday, the professor said that the exam would be cancelled.* |
| *People who run quickly burn calories.* | *People who **quickly** run burn calories.* (if "quickly" modifies "run") OR *People who run burn calories **quickly**.* (if "quickly" modifies "burn") |

---

## 6.5 — Comparative Analysis: Distinguishing the Three Types

Students frequently confuse dangling, misplaced, and squinting modifiers. The distinctions are critical:

| Feature | Dangling Modifier | Misplaced Modifier | Squinting Modifier |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Target present in sentence?** | ❌ No | ✅ Yes | ✅ Yes (two possible targets) |
| **Number of possible targets** | 0 | 1 (but wrong one is nearest) | 2 (both are grammatically possible) |
| **Nature of error** | No one in the sentence can perform the action | The right word is in the sentence but too far from the modifier | The modifier is between two words and could attach to either |
| **Typical position** | Introductory phrase | Anywhere | Between two modifiable words |
| **Correction strategy** | Add the missing subject OR rewrite as a clause | Move the modifier closer to its target | Reposition to one side to eliminate ambiguity |

### Side-by-Side Examples Using Similar Sentences

**Sentence 1 (Dangling):**
> *Running through the park, the sunset was beautiful.*

- "Running" has no one to attach to. The sunset is not running.
- Target: **absent**.

**Sentence 2 (Misplaced):**
> *Running through the park, the jogger watched the sunset.*

Wait — this is actually **correct**. "Running through the park" modifies "the jogger." The jogger is running. No error.

Let's create a misplaced version:
> *The jogger watched the sunset running through the park.*

- "Running through the park" is too far from "the jogger" and appears to modify "the sunset."
- Target: **present** ("the jogger") but **too far away**.

**Sentence 3 (Squinting):**
> *The jogger who ran quickly reached the finish line.*

- "Quickly" could modify "ran" (the jogger ran quickly) or "reached" (the jogger quickly reached).
- Actually, "quickly" is closer to "ran," so this is likely not squinting. Let's adjust:

> *Jogging quickly, the finish line was reached by the runner.*

- This is actually **dangling** (the finish line was not jogging).

A true squinting example:
> *The coach said during the game that the players would be substituted.*

- "During the game" could modify "said" (the coach said it during the game) or "would be substituted" (the players would be substituted during the game).
- **Two possible targets. Squinting.**

---

## 6.6 — Modifier Placement in SAT Answer Choices: How to Evaluate

When the SAT presents a sentence with a modifier and asks you to choose the correct version, the answer choices will typically offer **different placements** for the modifier or **different subjects** for the main clause. Here is your evaluation framework:

### Step 1: Identify the Modifier
Read the original sentence and locate the modifying word, phrase, or clause. Ask: "What is this supposed to describe?"

### Step 2: Identify the Intended Target
Based on context and logic, determine what the modifier is *supposed* to modify.

### Step 3: Check Each Answer Choice
For each choice, ask:
- Is the modifier next to its intended target?
- Does the modifier accidentally modify something else?
- Is the intended target even present in the sentence?
- If the modifier is between two words, could it modify both (squinting)?

### Step 4: Eliminate and Select
Eliminate choices that create dangling, misplaced, or squinting modifiers. Select the choice where the modifier is **unambiguously and correctly** attached to its intended target.

### Worked SAT-Style Example (Structural Analysis)

**Original sentence:**
> *Having practiced for months, the piano recital was performed flawlessly by the student.*

**Analysis:**
- Introductory phrase: "Having practiced for months"
- Action: Practicing
- Who practiced? The student.
- Subject of main clause: "the piano recital"
- Can a piano recital practice? No.
- **Error: Dangling modifier.**

**Hypothetical answer choices:**

*(A) Having practiced for months, the piano recital was performed flawlessly by the student.*
→ Same error. Dangling.

*(B) The piano recital was performed flawlessly by the student, having practiced for months.*
→ Still dangling. "Having practiced" is now at the end, but it still modifies the subject "the piano recital."

*(C) Having practiced for months, the student performed the piano recital flawlessly.*
→ "Having practiced for months" modifies "the student." The student practiced. ✅ **Correct.**

*(D) The student performed the piano recital flawlessly, having practiced for months.*
→ "Having practiced for months" is at the end. It modifies the subject "the student." This is also **correct** because end-of-sentence modifiers refer back to the subject.

Both (C) and (D) are grammatically correct. On the SAT, if both are offered, the choice between them depends on other factors (conciseness, style, or whether another rule is being tested simultaneously). However, if only one of these is offered, it is the correct answer.

---

## 6.7 — Advanced Modifier Placement Scenarios

### Scenario 1: Stacked Modifiers

Sometimes a sentence contains **multiple modifiers** in sequence, and the order matters.

> *The old wooden carved box sat on the shelf.*

Here, "old," "wooden," and "carved" all modify "box." But the order is important. "Carved" is a participle acting as an adjective, and it should be closest to the noun it modifies. "Old" is an opinion/age adjective, which comes before material adjectives.

Correct order: *The old carved wooden box sat on the shelf.*

The SAT is unlikely to test adjective ordering in this detail, but it **will** test whether a participial modifier is correctly placed relative to the noun it modifies.

### Scenario 2: Modifiers with Compound Subjects

> *Exhausted and hungry, the hikers finally reached the cabin.*

This is correct — "Exhausted and hungry" modifies "the hikers."

> *Exhausted and hungry, the cabin finally came into view.*

This is **dangling** — the cabin is not exhausted and hungry. The hikers are, but "the hikers" is not the subject.

**Correction:** *Exhausted and hungry, the hikers finally saw the cabin.*

### Scenario 3: Modifiers in Comparative Constructions

> *More than any other student, Maria studied the hardest.*

This is correct — "More than any other student" modifies "Maria" (by implication, Maria is being compared to other students).

> *Maria studied harder than any student in the class.*

Wait — "any student" includes Maria herself. This is an **illogical comparison** (covered in Chapter 11 of the full guide), but it also relates to modifier placement because the comparative phrase "than any student" must be positioned to create a logical comparison.

**Correction:** *Maria studied harder than **any other** student in the class.*

### Scenario 4: Modifiers with Negatives

> *She didn't barely finish the race.*

This is a **double negative**, not strictly a modifier placement error, but the placement of "barely" relative to "didn't" creates confusion.

**Correction:** *She **barely** didn't finish the race.* (ambiguous) → Better: *She **barely** finished the race.* OR *She **didn't** finish the race.*

### Scenario 5: Modifiers in Passive Constructions

Passive voice is the **primary enabler** of dangling modifiers because it removes the agent (the doer) from the subject position.

> *After being carefully reviewed, the committee approved the proposal.*

Wait — who reviewed? The committee? Then "After being carefully reviewed" modifies "the committee," but the committee is doing the reviewing, not being reviewed. This is actually **dangling** if the intended meaning is that someone *else* reviewed the proposal.

If the committee reviewed the proposal:
> *After carefully reviewing **the proposal**, the committee approved it.*

If someone else reviewed it:
> *After **being** carefully reviewed **by the external board**, the proposal was approved by the committee.*

The SAT frequently tests this pattern. **Whenever you see a passive construction with an introductory participial phrase, check for a dangling modifier.**

---

## 6.8 — The Complete Modifier Placement Checklist

Use this checklist when evaluating any sentence on the SAT for modifier errors:

### Dangling Modifier Checklist
- [ ] Does the sentence begin with an introductory phrase (especially one ending in *-ing*, *-ed*, or *to + verb*)?
- [ ] Can I identify who or what performs the action in that phrase?
- [ ] Is that person or thing the **subject** of the main clause?
- [ ] If the main clause uses passive voice, is the agent (doer) in the subject position?
- [ ] If the answer to any of the above is "no," the modifier dangles.

### Misplaced Modifier Checklist
- [ ] Can I identify the modifier (word, phrase, or clause)?
- [ ] Can I identify the word it is **intended** to modify?
- [ ] Is the modifier placed **as close as possible** to that word?
- [ ] Does the modifier accidentally appear to modify a **different** word (usually the nearest noun)?
- [ ] If the modifier is "only," "almost," "nearly," or a similar limiting adverb, does its position match the intended meaning?
- [ ] If the modifier is at the end of the sentence, does it correctly refer back to the **subject**?

### Squinting Modifier Checklist
- [ ] Is the modifier positioned **between** two words that it could logically modify?
- [ ] Does the sentence have **two possible meanings** depending on which word the modifier attaches to?
- [ ] Can I reposition the modifier to **one side** to eliminate the ambiguity?

---

## 6.9 — Common SAT Trap Patterns

Understanding the patterns the SAT uses to create modifier errors will help you identify them faster.

### Trap 1: The "By" Prepositional Phrase
> *By studying hard, good grades are achieved.*

- "By studying hard" is a prepositional phrase with a gerund. Who studies? Not "good grades."
- **Dangling.** Correction: *By studying hard, **students** achieve good grades.*

### Trap 2: The "After" Clause
> *After finishing dinner, the dishes were washed.*

- Who finished dinner? Not the dishes.
- **Dangling.** Correction: *After finishing dinner, **we** washed the dishes.*

### Trap 3: The "To" Infinitive
> *To pass the exam, studying is necessary.*

- Who needs to pass? Not "studying."
- **Dangling.** Correction: *To pass the exam, **you** need to study.*

### Trap 4: The "Being" Participial
> *Being a cold day, the fire was lit.*

- Who is cold? Not the fire. The day is cold, but "the day" is inside a prepositional phrase ("Being a cold day"), not the subject.
- **Dangling.** Correction: *Because **it was** a cold day, the fire was lit.* OR *Being a cold day, **we** lit the fire.*

### Trap 5: The "Having + Past Participle" Phrase
> *Having been elected, the president's policies were implemented.*

- Who was elected? The president. But "the president" is in a possessive form ("president's"), not the subject. The subject is "policies," which cannot be elected.
- **Dangling.** Correction: *Having been elected, **the president** implemented new policies.* OR *After **the president** was elected, new policies were implemented.*

### Trap 6: The "Which" Clause at a Distance
> *The report was submitted to the manager which was incomplete.*

- "Which was incomplete" is too far from "report" and appears to modify "manager."
- **Misplaced.** Correction: *The report, **which was incomplete**, was submitted to the manager.* OR *The **incomplete** report was submitted to the manager.*

### Trap 7: The "Only" Shift
> *The company only produces three models.*

If the intended meaning is that the company produces **only three** models (not more), then "only" should be before "three":
> *The company produces **only** three models.*

If the intended meaning is that producing three models is the company's **sole activity**, then the original placement is acceptable.

---

## 6.10 — Modifier Placement and Sentence Logic

Beyond the grammatical rules, modifier placement is fundamentally about **logic**. A sentence must make sense not just grammatically but conceptually. When evaluating modifier placement, always ask:

1. **Does the sentence describe a physically possible scenario?** (A couch cannot be exhausted. A sunset cannot run.)
2. **Does the sentence describe a logically coherent scenario?** (Can a building be "thinking"? Can a book "read" someone?)
3. **Does the sentence describe the scenario the author intended?** (If the author meant that the *student* was nervous, the sentence should not say that the *test* was nervous.)

The SAT's modifier questions are ultimately testing your ability to ensure that **description matches reality**. A modifier that attaches to the wrong noun creates an impossible or absurd image — and the SAT expects you to catch it.

---

## 6.11 — Modifier Placement in Context: Paragraph-Level Analysis
